Salah — Prayer
beginner
8 min
Sunnahs of Bowing, Prostration, and Sitting
Enumerates sunnah acts of bowing, prostration, and sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the sunnah acts related to bowing, prostration, and sitting, and distinguish between the postures for men and women.

Core Idea
Sunnahs of bowing, prostration, and sitting complete the prayer and reflect the Prophet's ﷺ guidance.

The prayer includes many recommended acts in bowing, prostration, and sitting that ensure the worshiper follows the Sunnah closely. This lesson covers items 18-39, including the takbirs, tasbih, physical postures, and differences between men and women. Mastering these sunnahs brings the prayer to its full beauty and reward.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
Group the items: bowing (18-22), prostration (23-34), sitting and between prostrations (35-39).
2
List bowing sunnahs
Items 18-22: takbir for bowing, tasbih thrice, grasping knees, spreading fingers (men only), straightening shanks. Also flatten back and level head with posterior (23-24).
3
List prostration sunnahs
Items 25-34: rising from bowing, standing upright, placing knees then hands then face, takbir for prostration and rising, prostration between palms, tasbih thrice, mujafat for men, compact for women.
4
List sitting sunnahs
Items 35-39: qawma (standing after prostration), sitting between prostrations, hand placement on thighs, iftirash for men (left foot under, right foot vertical), tawarruk for women.
5
Note gender differences
Women do not spread fingers in bowing (item 21), are compact in prostration (34), and sit mutawarrikan (39). Men spread fingers and do mujafat.

In its sunnahs: 10 - And saying 'al-hamdu lillah'. 11 - And being quiet with it. 12 - And standing straight at the takbir, without bowing the head. 13 and 14 - And the imam's raising his voice with the takbir and tasmi'. 15 - And separating the feet in standing to the extent of four fingers. 16 - And that the surah attached to al-Fatiha be: a) from the long mufassal in Fajr and Zuhr; b) from its middle in Asr and Isha; c) from its short ones in Maghrib if he is a resident; d) any surah he wishes if he is a traveler. 17 - And lengthening the first (standing) in Fajr only. 18 - And the takbir of bowing. 19 - And his tasbih (glorification) thrice. 20 - And taking his knees with his hands. 21 - And spreading his fingers, and the woman does not spread them. 22 - And straightening his shanks. (1) That is, with all that was mentioned of praise and what follows. (2) From al-Hujurat to al-Buruj; its middle from al-Buruj to al-Bayyinah; its short ones from al-Bayyinah to the end of the Noble Qur'an; it is called mufassal because of the many separations between its verses and surahs. (From Mīrqāt). In its sunnahs: 23 - And flattening his back. 24 - And making his head level with his posterior. 25 and 26 - And rising from bowing, and standing upright afterward. 27 - And placing his knees, then his hands, then his face for prostration. 28 - And the reverse for rising up. 29 - And the takbir of prostration. 30 - And the takbir of rising from it. 31 - And that the prostration be between his palms. 32 - And his tasbih thrice. 33 - And the man holding his stomach away from his thighs, his elbows away from his sides, and his forearms away from the ground. 34 - And the woman lowering herself and pressing her stomach to her thighs. 35 - And the qawma (standing after rising from prostration). 36 - And the sitting between the two prostrations. 37 - And placing the hands on the thighs in the sitting between the two prostrations like the state of tashahhud. 38 - And stretching his left foot and standing the right foot. 39 - And the woman sitting mutawarrikan. (1) And the woman stretches her forearms. (Ibn Abidin 3/345). (2) That is, complete standing. (3) So she brings her legs out from her right side and makes her hip rest on the ground. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/34).
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage enumerates the sunnah acts of the prayer, which are recommended practices that complete and beautify the obligatory prayer. The list begins with saying 'al-hamdu lillah' (tahmid) after the opening supplication, and keeping the recitation quiet when not being heard. It then mentions standing straight at the opening takbir without bowing the head, the imam's audible takbir and tasmi' (sami'a allahu liman hamidah), and separating the feet about four finger-widths. A significant sunnah is choosing the appropriate surah length from the mufassal (the last section of the Qur'an) according to the prayer: long in Fajr and Zuhr, medium in Asr and Isha, and short in Maghrib for a resident; a traveler may recite any surah. The first rak'ah in Fajr should be lengthened. During bowing, the takbir is said, followed by three tasbihs, and the knees are grasped with hands while fingers are spread (men only; women keep them together) and shanks are made straight. The back is flattened and the head aligns with the back. After bowing, one rises and stands upright, then prostrates placing knees, hands, and face, and the prostration is done between the hands. Tasbih is repeated thrice in each prostration. Men hold their stomach away from thighs and elbows away from sides, while women keep a compact posture. The sitting between prostrations is done with the left foot underneath and right foot vertical for men; women sit mutawarrikan (legs to the right). These sunnahs ensure the prayer follows the example of the Prophet ﷺ and are not obligatory, but their omission deprives one of great reward.

Key Terms
التورك (al-tawarruk): A sitting posture where the woman sits with legs out to the right side, resting hip on the ground.
القومة (al-qawma): Standing upright after rising from prostration before the next posture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the woman’s sunnah posture in sitting between the two prostrations?
A) Iftirash (sitting on left foot, right foot vertical)
B) Tawarruk (sitting on the floor with legs to the right)
C) Squatting
D) Kneeling with back straight

Key Takeaway
The sunnahs of bowing, prostration, and sitting include specific postures and recitations, with key differences between men and women for modesty and following the Sunnah.

Salah — Prayer

The Sunan of Prayer: Hanafi Recommended Acts (23-51)
Enumeration of 29 sunan acts of prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 29 sunan acts of prayer listed in Nur al-Idah and explain that omitting them does not invalidate the prayer.

Core Idea
Sunan perfect prayer but are not obligatory; habitual omission is blameworthy, yet no compensation (e.g. sujud al-sahw) is required.

The Hanafi school emphasizes that prayer is built on four categories: fard, wajib, sunnah, and mustahabb. The sunnah acts, though not obligatory, complete the beauty and perfection of the prayer. The following passage from Nur al-Idah enumerates 29 such acts from the moment of bowing to the final taslim. Mastering these details distinguishes the careful worshipper from the negligent one.

Guided Path
1
Categorize by posture
Group the sunan into bowing, prostration, sitting, taslim, and intention. This helps in memorization and application.
e.g., items 23-28 relate to bowing; 27-34 to prostration; 35-39 to sitting; 44-51 to taslim and intention.
2
Identify gender-specific rulings
Note differences: women lower themselves (34), sit in tawarruk (39), while men spread legs and keep belly away (33, 38).
These differences are based on the principle of satr (concealment) and comfort.
3
Distinguish sunnah from wajib
The passage only lists sunan. In Hanafi fiqh, omitting a sunnah does not require sujud al-sahw, but omitting a wajib (like the final tashahhud) does.
Check your prior knowledge: Is tashahhud sunnah or wajib? The passage mentions 'tashahhud' only in context of hand placement (37) and finger pointing (40).
4
Memorize the list in order
Use the numbered list (23-51) as a mnemonic. Recite the acts in sequence during prayer review.
Start from item 23 (Bast zahrihi – spreading the back) and go through to item 51 (intizar al-masbuq – latecomer waiting).
5
Apply intentions for taslim
Note that imam, follower, and individual have different intentions for the taslim (45-47). This ensures the salam reaches the intended recipients.
The imam intends men, guardian angels, and righteous jinn; the follower intends the same plus the imam; the individual intends only angels.

In its sunan: 23 - Spreading his back. 24 - Leveling his head with his posterior. 25, 26 - Rising from bowing, and standing thereafter with tranquility. 27 - Placing his knees, then his hands, then his face for prostration. 28 - The opposite for rising. 29 - The takbir of prostration. 30 - The takbir of rising from it. 31 - That the prostration be between his palms. 32 - His glorification, three times. 33 - The man distancing his belly from his thighs, his elbows from his sides, and his forearms from the ground. 34 - The woman lowering herself and sticking her belly to her thighs. 35 - The standing upright. 36 - The sitting between the two prostrations. 37 - Placing the hands on the thighs during the sitting between the two prostrations as in the state of tashahhud. 38 - Spreading his left leg and erecting the right. 39 - The woman sitting in tawarruk. 40 - Pointing - in the correct view - with the index finger at the shahada, raising it at negation and lowering it at affirmation. 41 - Reciting al-Fatiha after the first two rak'ahs. 42 - Sending blessings upon the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, in the final sitting. 43 - Supplication with words resembling the Qur'an and Sunnah, not the speech of people. 44 - Turning right, then left, with the two taslims. 45 - The intention of the imam: a) men, b) the guardian angels, c) righteous jinn, with the two taslims, in the most correct view. 46 - The intention of the follower for his imam in his direction, and if he is opposite him: he intends him in the two taslims along with the people, the guardian angels, and righteous jinn. 47 - The intention of the one praying alone for only the angels. 48 - Lowering the second taslim more than the first. 49 - His synchronization with the imam's salam. 50 - Beginning with the right. 51 - The latecomer waiting for the imam to finish.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage enumerates the sunan (recommended acts) of the prayer according to the Hanafi school. These are acts that complete and beautify the prayer but are not obligatory; omitting them does not invalidate the prayer, though it is blameworthy to leave them habitually. The list covers various postures: the back and head position in bowing, the sequence of limbs in prostration, the takbirs, the dhikr, the positioning of limbs for men vs. women, the sitting postures, the pointing finger in tashahhud, recitation, supplication, the taslim, and the intentions for imam, follower, and individual. The author notes specific rulings: a woman sits in tawarruk, her belly sticks to thighs, and she lowers herself more; the pointing finger is raised at 'la ilaha' and lowered at 'illallah'; the imam intends men, guardian angels, and righteous jinn; the follower intends his imam and those groups; the individual intends only angels; the second taslim is softer; the latecomer waits for the imam's completion. These details ensure the prayer is performed in the most perfect manner.

Key Terms
السنة (al-sunnah): A recommended act in prayer that is not obligatory; omitting it is not sinful but leaves the prayer less perfect.
التورك (al-tawarruk): A sitting posture for women in prayer where she sits on her left buttock with both legs out to the right.
المسبحة (al-musabbihah): The index finger, used for pointing during tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the sunnah for a woman's prostration?
A) She spreads her arms like a man.
B) She lowers herself and sticks her belly to her thighs.
C) She keeps her elbows away from her sides.
D) She raises her head and back together.

Key Takeaway
The 29 sunan of prayer, from bowing posture to intention in taslim, complete the perfection of the prayer but are not obligatory; omitting them does not invalidate the prayer.

Sunan of Salah (40-51): Gesture, Recitation, Greeting, and Intentions
Listing sunan of salah from 40 to 51

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the remaining sunan of salah from 40 to 51 and explain the different intentions during taslim.

Core Idea
These twelve sunan perfect the prayer; intention during taslim includes men, angels, and righteous jinn for the imam.

This passage continues the enumeration of sunan (recommended acts) in prayer. It covers the pointing gesture during tashahhud, recitation of al-Fatihah in later rak'ahs, sending salawat, supplication, turning for taslim, and the specific intentions for the imam, follower, and individual. These sunan enhance the prayer's completeness and are not wajib, but their practice reflects following the Prophet's ﷺ example.

Guided Path
1
List the sunan in order
Read items 40 to 51 sequentially, noting each sunnah. Focus on the gesture (40), recitation (41-43), turning (44), intentions (45-47), volume (48-49), order (50), and waiting (51).
Use the numbering to memorize the sequence.
2
Distinguish the pointing gesture
Item 40: raise the index finger (musabbihah) at negation (la ilaha) and lower at affirmation (illa Allah). This is sunnah, not wajib.
Some schools differ on the exact timing.
3
Analyze the intentions for taslim
Items 45-47: the imam intends men, guardian angels, and righteous jinn; the follower intends the imam, congregation, angels, and jinn; the individual intends angels only.
The phrase 'in the most correct view' (al-ashahh) indicates a preferred Hanafi position.
4
Observe the order and volume of taslim
Item 48: lower the second taslim more than the first. Items 49-50: the follower should conform to the imam and begin with the right side.
The taslim itself is wajib, but these details are sunnah.
5
Note the masbuq's action
Item 51: the masbuq (latecomer) should wait for the imam to complete both taslims before standing to make up missed rak'ahs.
This ensures proper sequence for the follower.

Concerning its sunan: 40. Pointing – in the correct view – with the index finger during the testimony, raising it at the negation (la ilaha) and lowering it at the affirmation (illa Allah). 41. Reciting al-Fatihah in the [rak'ahs] after the first two. 42. Sending prayers upon the Prophet (peace be upon him) in the last sitting. 43. Supplicating with what resembles the wording of the Quran and Sunnah, not the speech of people. 44. Turning the head right then left with the two taslims. 45. The intention of the imam: men, guardian angels, and righteous jinn with the two taslims, in the most correct view. 46. The intention of the follower for his imam in his direction, and if opposite: he intends him with the two taslims along with the congregation, guardian angels, and righteous jinn. 47. The intention of the one praying alone for the angels only. 48. Lowering the second [taslim] more than the first. 49. His conformity to the imam's taslim. 50. Beginning with the right [taslim]. 51. The masbuq waiting for the imam to finish. (1) Until the whiteness of his cheek is seen. (2) i.e., the second taslim.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage lists a series of sunan (recommended acts) in prayer, continuing from previous pages. Item 40: raising the index finger at tashahhud, lifting it at 'la ilaha' (negation) and lowering it at 'illa Allah' (affirmation). This is a sunnah gesture, not wajib. Item 41: reciting Surah al-Fatihah in the third and fourth rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer is sunnah; one may also recite another surah. Item 42: sending salawat on the Prophet in the final tashahhud. Item 43: making dua with Quranic or prophetic phrases, avoiding ordinary speech. Item 44: turning the head right then left while saying the two taslims; the taslim itself is wajib, but the slight turn is sunnah. Items 45-47: the intention (niyyah) when giving taslim: the imam intends the congregation, angels, and righteous jinn; the follower intends the imam and congregation; the individual intends angels only. Item 48: the second taslim should be lower in volume than the first. Item 49: the follower should make taslim immediately after the imam. Item 50: begin with the right taslim. Item 51: the masbuq (latecomer) should wait for the imam to finish taslim before standing to complete his missed rak'ahs. These sunan enhance the prayer but omitting them does not invalidate it.

Key Terms
التسليمتان (al-taslimatayn): The two greetings of peace (saying 'Assalamu alaykum wa rahmatullah') at the end of prayer: one to the right and one to the left.
المسبوق (al-masbuq): A person who joins the congregation after the imam has already started some rak'ahs, and thus misses part of the prayer.
المُسبحة (al-musabbihah): The index finger used for pointing during tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the sunnah regarding the volume of the second taslim compared to the first?
A) It should be louder.
B) It should be equal.
C) It should be lower.
D) It should be whispered.

Key Takeaway
These twelve sunan refine the prayer; the most nuanced are the intentions during taslim, which vary by the worshiper's role.

Adab of Salah: Six Recommended Etiquettes
Introducing the etiquettes (adab) of prayer with six specific actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six adab of salah and state the appropriate gaze for each posture.

Core Idea
Adab are mustahabb acts that perfect prayer's decorum; omitting them does not invalidate the salah.

This passage introduces the etiquettes (adab) of prayer—recommended behaviors that enhance the spiritual quality and outward decorum. Unlike fard or wajib, these are not obligatory, but observing them reflects devotion and respect. We will examine each of the six items and their practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six adab
Read through items 1-6 in the Arabic text and translation. Note each adab: removing hands from sleeves, specific gazes, suppressing cough, controlling yawn, standing at 'Hayya ala al-falah', imam's start timing.
All six are distinct acts; some have sub-details (gaze has five postures).
2
Associate gaze directions with postures
Memorize the gaze rule for each posture: standing → place of prostration, bowing → top of feet, prostrating → tip of nose, sitting → lap, greeting (salam) → shoulders.
This is a memorization anchor for the student.
3
Understand the ruling level (mustahabb)
Recognize that these are adab, which are mustahabb (recommended). Omitting them does not affect the validity of the prayer but reduces its perfection.
Contrast with sunnah muakkadah which has more emphasis.
4
Apply the adab in practice
Implement these adab in your daily prayers. For example, when you feel a yawn, bite your lower lip or cover with the back of your left hand.
The adab are practical; practice them consistently.

140. From its adab: Chapter on the Adab of Prayer. 1. The man taking out his two hands from his sleeves at the takbir (i.e., takbirat al-ihram). 2. The gaze of the one praying: a) to his place of prostration while standing; b) to the top of his foot while bowing; c) to the tip of his nose while prostrating; d) to his lap while sitting; e) to his shoulders while greeting. 3. Suppressing coughing as much as possible. 4. Controlling his mouth when yawning (2) (i.e., by biting his lower lip, otherwise covering with back of left hand). 5. Standing up when 'Hayya ala al-falah' is said. 6. The imam starting when 'Qad qamat al-salah' is said (3) (i.e., the imam beginning his ihram, though delaying until it is completed is better). Footnotes: (1) i.e., takbirat al-ihram. (2) Even by biting his lower lip; if unable, cover with the back of his left hand – Ibn Abidin 3/252. (3) i.e., the imam starting his ihram; if he delays until it is fully recited, it is better – Ibn Abidin 3/256.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the adab (etiquettes) of prayer. These are recommended behaviors that enhance the spiritual quality and outward decorum of the prayer. Item 1: men should remove their hands from sleeves when raising them for takbirat al-ihram. Item 2: the worshipper's gaze should be directed to specific places depending on posture: to the place of prostration while standing, to the top of the foot while bowing, to the tip of the nose while prostrating, to the lap while sitting, and to the shoulders when turning for taslim. Item 3: suppress coughing as much as possible; if it overcomes, it does not break the prayer but is against adab. Item 4: when yawning, one should control the mouth, either by biting the lower lip or covering with the back of the left hand. Item 5: when the muadhdhin says 'Hayya ala al-falah', those waiting for congregational prayer should stand up. Item 6: the imam should begin the takbir when the iqamah reaches 'Qad qamat al-salah'; however, it is better to delay until the iqamah is fully completed before starting, according to a relied-upon note from Ibn Abidin. These adab are all mustahabb (recommended); neglecting them does not invalidate the prayer but reduces its perfection.

Key Terms
التكبيرة (al-takbirah): Here it refers to takbirat al-ihram, the opening takbir of prayer.
قد قامت الصلاة (qad qamat al-salah): The phrase in the iqamah indicating that the prayer is about to begin.
الآداب (al-adab): A set of recommended etiquettes that elevate the quality of worship beyond the obligatory and recommended acts.

Key Takeaway
The six adab of salah are mustahabb (recommended) acts that perfect the prayer's outer decorum; their omission does not invalidate the prayer.

Actions of Entering Prayer (First Rak'ah)
Describes the actions of entering prayer: hands, takbir, intention, valid starting, hand placement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended and obligatory actions when entering prayer (first rak'ah) in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Entering prayer requires takbir with intention, raising hands to ears, and placing right hand over left below navel immediately.

The first rak'ah begins with specific physical actions that set the tone for prayer. This lesson walks through the steps of removing hands from sleeves, raising them, saying takbir with intention, and hand placement as per Hanafi fiqh. Understanding the correct sequence and their rulings ensures a valid and proper start to salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Actions
List the actions described in the passage: taking hands out of sleeves, raising hands, saying takbir, making intention, starting prayer (shuru'), and placing right hand over left below navel.
The passage focuses on the first rak'ah only.
2
Classify Each Action's Ruling
For each action, determine whether it is wajib, sunnah, or a condition. For example, takbirat al-ihram is wajib, raising hands is sunnah, intention is a condition (shart).
Refer to the footnote for hand placement: below navel is the Hanafi position.
3
Note Gender Differences
The footnote specifies that women raise hands to the level of the chest, not ears. Apply this distinction when describing the actions.
This shows precision in Hanafi rulings for men vs women.
4
Sequence and Timing
The actions must be performed in order: raise hands, say takbir with intention, then after tahrimah, place hands immediately without delay. Emphasize the immediacy of hand placement.
'Bila muhlah' – without delay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

في كيفية تركيب الصلاة
الركعة الأولى :]
إذا أراد الدخول في الصلاة:
١ - أخرج كَفَّيه من كُمِّيه، ثم رَفَعَهُما حِذاءَ أَذنَيْه(۱).
٢- ثم كبر بلا مد(٢)، ناوياً.
- ويصح الشروع:
أ ـ بكل ذكر خالص الله تعالى، كـ: سبحان الله.
ب ـ وبالفارسية إن عجز عن العربية كالقراءة بها للعاجز عن العربية.
ج - وإن قَدَرَ على العربية : لا يصح شروعه بالفارسية (۳)، ولا قراءته بها، في الأصح.
مهلة.
٤- ثم وضع يمينه على يساره تحت سُرَّته، عقب التحريمة، بلا
(1) أما المرأة فترفع إلى حذاء صدرها.
(۲) للهمز .
(۳) حرر ابن عابدین ۲۷۳/۳ أن الشروع بالفارسية صحيح وإن قدر علـى
العربية، وهو قول الإمام، وإليه رجع الصاحبان.
Chapter on the composition of prayer. The first rak'ah: When he intends to enter prayer: 1. He takes out his two palms from his sleeves, then raises them to the level of his ears (As for the woman, she raises to the level of her chest). 2. Then he says takbir without prolongation (because of the hamzah), with intention. 3. And the commencement is valid: a) with any dhikr purely for Allah, like 'Subhanallah'; b) and with Persian if he is unable to (speak) Arabic, like recitation with it for one unable to Arabic; c) and if he is able to Arabic, his commencement with Persian is not valid, nor his recitation with it, according to the most correct view. (Ibn Abidin 3/273 clarified that commencement with Persian is valid even if he is able to Arabic, and that is the saying of the Imam, and to it the two companions retracted.) 4. Then he places his right hand on his left below his navel, immediately after the tahrimah, without delay.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage details the actions of the first rak'ah upon entering prayer. First, the person removes his hands from his sleeves—a recommended act—and raises them to the ears (men) or chest (women, per footnote). Second, he says the takbir (Allahu Akbar) without prolonging the vowel because of the hamzah, ensuring correct pronunciation. This takbir is made with the intention of entering prayer. Third, the commencement (shuru') of prayer can be made with any dhikr dedicated solely to Allah, like 'Subhanallah', or with Persian for one unable to speak Arabic (analogous to reciting Quran in Persian for those who cannot). However, the text states that if one is capable of Arabic, commencing in Persian is not valid according to the most correct view (fi al-ashahh). But the footnote from Ibn Abidin corrects this: Imam Abu Hanifa held that it is valid even if one can speak Arabic, and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) originally disagreed but later retracted to the Imam's position. Thus, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that commencing with Persian is valid. This is an important intra-Hanafi correction. Fourth, after the takbirat al-ihram (tahrimah), the worshipper places his right hand over his left below the navel immediately, without delay. This is the Hanafi position for hand placement.

Key Terms
تحريمة (tahrimah): The takbirat al-ihram that makes prayer forbidden (haram) for other actions; the entrance into prayer.
شروع (shuru'): The act of commencing or entering into prayer, which can be done with various utterances.
الفارسية (al-farisiyyah): The Persian language, permissible for shuru' and Quran recitation for those unable to Arabic, and even for those able according to the mufta bihi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She raises them to her ears
B) She raises them to her chest
C) She does not raise them
D) She raises them to her shoulders

Key Takeaway
Entering prayer requires the wajib takbir with intention, followed by sunnah acts of raising hands (ears for men, chest for women) and placing hands below navel immediately.

Validity of Starting Prayer in Persian for Arabic Speakers
Describes the actions of entering prayer: hands, takbir, intention, valid starting, hand placement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on starting prayer in Persian for Arabic speakers and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The mufta bihi is that starting prayer in Persian is valid even if you can speak Arabic, due to Imam Abu Hanifa's position with Sahibayn's retraction.

The passage initially states that starting prayer in Persian is invalid for Arabic speakers ('fi al-ashahh'), but a footnote from Ibn Abidin corrects this: Imam Abu Hanifa ruled it valid, and the two senior disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) retracted their opposition. This lesson explores the nature of this intra-Hanafi correction and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling in the Main Text
Read step 3c: 'If he is able to Arabic, his commencement with Persian is not valid, nor his recitation with it, according to the most correct view.' Note that this is the asahh (most correct) but not necessarily the mufta bihi.
The phrase 'fi al-ashahh' indicates a strong view but not definitively the final fatwa.
2
Examine the Footnote Correction
The footnote from Ibn Abidin (3/273) states: 'commencement with Persian is valid even if he is able to Arabic' and attributes this to Imam Abu Hanifa, adding that the Sahibayn retracted.
This is a critical expansion (expansion_probe): the footnote overrides the text's statement.
3
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa held validity; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) originally held invalidity but later retracted. The footnote makes clear the retraction, so the unified position is validity.
The retraction is significant because it elevates the Imam's view to the mufta bihi.
4
Apply the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon ruling is that a person able to Arabic may validly start prayer with Persian (or any dhikr in Persian). This applies to shuru' (commencement) and also to Quran recitation if needed? The text says 'reading' as well, but the footnote focuses on shuru'.
Note: The ruling on recitation in Persian for those able to Arabic may have a similar dispute but the passage only mentions shuru'.

Steps of the First Rak'ah in Hanafi Prayer
Instructional sequence for the first rak'ah of prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recite the sequence of actions in the first rak'ah of prayer and explain the Hanafi ruling on starting prayer in Persian.

Core Idea
The first rak'ah sequence from takbir to sujud includes specific placements and recitations; Persian is only allowed for the incapable in the most correct view.

This lesson walks through the thirteen steps of the first rak'ah as outlined in Nur al-Idah. You will learn the correct postures, recitations, and key differences between the imam, follower, and latecomer. A notable intra-Hanafi dispute about using Persian to start the prayer is also examined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opening actions
Focus on how the worshipper enters prayer: removing hands from sleeves, raising them (men to ears, women to chest), and saying the takbir with intention.
The takbir is not prolonged; the hamzah is pronounced sharply.
2
Examine the conditions for a valid start
Starting with any pure dhikr (e.g., 'Subhan Allah') is valid. Using Persian is only valid for those unable to use Arabic; otherwise it is invalid according to the relied-upon view.
A footnote indicates Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples later held it valid even if capable.
3
Trace the recitation sequence
After takbir, recite the opening supplication (istiftah: 'Subhanaka Allahumma...'), then seek refuge (ta'awwudh) silently, then basmalah, then al-Fatihah, then a surah (or three verses). All recitations are silent.
The ta'awwudh is for the latecomer but not the follower; it is delayed after Eid takbirs.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on Persian
The main text says starting in Persian is invalid if capable of Arabic ('al-ashah'). The footnote by Ibn Abidin states that Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (after retraction) considered it valid. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
The tarjih (preference) may be the Imam's view, but the text presents the other as the most correct.
5
Summarize the bowing and prostration steps
After recitation, say takbir and bow (ruku') with tranquility, making head level with back, grasping knees, and saying 'Subhana rabbiyal adheem' at least three times. Rise saying 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah...' (imam or alone; follower only says the praise). Then say takbir and prostrate: place knees first, then hands.
There is a difference of opinion on saying basmalah between al-Fatihah and the surah; see Ibn Abidin.

The Manner of Constructing the Prayer: The First Rak'ah. Section: On the Manner of Constructing the Prayer. When one intends to enter the prayer: 1- He takes out his hands from his sleeves, then raises them level with his ears. (1) 2- Then he says takbir without prolonging (the hamzah), intending (the prayer). - And starting (the prayer) is valid: a- With any dhikr that is purely for Allah Almighty, such as 'Subhanallah'. b- And in Persian if he is unable (to do it) in Arabic, like reciting (the Quran) in Persian for the one unable to do it in Arabic. c- But if he is able (to do it) in Arabic, his starting (the prayer) in Persian is not valid, nor his reciting (the Quran) in it, according to the most correct opinion. (3) (A pause?) 4- Then he places his right hand over his left under his navel, after the takbir al-tahrimah, without (?) (1) As for the woman, she raises her hands to the level of her chest. (2) Because of the hamzah. (3) Ibn Abidin (3/273) clarified that starting (the prayer) in Persian is valid even if he is able to do it in Arabic, and it is the saying of the Imam (Abu Hanifa), and the two companions retracted to it. On the Manner of Constructing the Prayer. He (the one praying) as one opening (the prayer), says: 'Subhanaka Allahumma wa bihamdika, wa tabaraka ismuka, wa ta'ala jadduka, wa la ilaha ghayruka.' And every person praying opens (with this). 6- Then he seeks refuge (ta'awwudh) silently for the recitation; the latecomer (masbuq) says it, not the follower (muqtadi), and it is delayed (1) after the takbirs of the two Eids. 7- Then he says the basmalah silently, and he says the basmalah in every rak'ah only before al-Fatihah. Then he recites al-Fatihah, and the imam and the follower say 'Ameen' silently. 9- Then he recites a surah, or three verses. 10- Then he says takbir while bowing: a- with tranquility, b- making his head level with his back, c- taking his knees with his hands, d- separating his fingers (2) e- and he glorifies (says subhanallah) three times, and that is the minimum. 11- Then he raises his head, and stands up tranquilly saying: 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah, Rabbana laka al-hamd', if he is an imam or praying alone. The follower is content with just the tahmid. 12- Then he says takbir while falling down for prostration. 13- Then he places: a- his knees. (1) i.e., the ta'awwudh. (2) And there is no harm in the basmalah. There is difference of opinion regarding that. b- then his hands. Between al-Fatihah and the surah. See Ibn Abidin (3/294), and he mentioned.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an instructional ruling on the sequence of actions in the first rak'ah of prayer. It details thirteen steps from the initial takbir to prostration. The text provides the standard Hanafi ruling but includes important nuances: women raise their hands to chest height, the takbir is not prolonged, and starting the prayer with Persian is valid only if one cannot do it in Arabic (though a footnote clarifies that Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples considered it valid even if capable). The opening supplication (subhanaka) is recited by every person praying. Seeking refuge (ta'awwudh) is for recitation and is said by the latecomer but not by the follower, and it is delayed after the Eid takbirs. The basmalah is said silently before al-Fatihah in every rak'ah. After al-Fatihah, the imam and follower say 'Ameen' silently. Then a surah or three verses are recited. Bowing (ruku') requires tranquility, level head and back, grasping knees, and at minimum three glorifications. Rising from bowing includes the imam or alone saying the appropriate phrases; the follower only says the tahmid. Prostration begins with a takbir and placing knees first, then hands. The text also notes a difference of opinion on saying basmalah between al-Fatihah and the surah, referring to Ibn Abidin for more.

Key Terms
التحريمة (al-tahrimah): The opening takbir (saying 'Allahu Akbar') that prohibits actions outside the prayer.
الاستفتاح (al-istiftah): The supplication recited immediately after the opening takbir, beginning with 'Subhanaka Allahumma...'
التعوذ (al-ta'awwudh): Seeking refuge with Allah from Shaytan before reciting the Quran, by saying 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytan al-rajim'.
التسمية (al-tasmiyah): Saying 'Bismillahir Rahmanir Rahim' before reciting al-Fatihah.
ركوع (ruku'): Bowing in prayer; the act of bending at the waist with hands on knees.
سجود (sujud): Prostration; placing the forehead, nose, hands, knees, and toes on the ground.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Level with her ears
B) Level with her shoulders
C) Level with her chest
D) Level with her navel

Key Takeaway
The first rak'ah of prayer follows a specific sequence; knowing the rulings on each step ensures valid prayer, especially regarding the language of the opening takbir.

Salah — Prayer
beginner
6 min
Sequence of Prayer Actions: Thanā' to Second Rak'ah
Step-by-step description of physical actions of salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sequence of actions in the first and second rak'ahs of salah and identify which are sunnah, wajib, or fard.

Core Idea
The prayer's actions are ordered: thanā', ta'awwudh, basmalah, Fatihah, surah, ruku', sujood, each with defined sunnah details.

This lesson walks you through the prescribed actions of salah from the opening supplication (thanā') to the start of the second rak'ah. These are the practical steps every Hanafi must know to perform prayer correctly. Pay attention to which actions are sunnah and which are wajib, as missing a wajib requires compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting point after takbirat al-ihram
The prayer begins with the opening supplication (thanā')—'Subhanaka Allahumma…'—which is sunnah for every person praying. Note that this is recited after the takbir, not before.
This thanā' is specific to the first rak'ah only.
2
List the recitations before al-Fatihah
After thanā', the worshiper recites ta'awwudh (seeking refuge) quietly, but only if he is not a follower (muqtadi). Then he says basmalah quietly, then recites al-Fatihah. The imam and follower say 'Amin' quietly after the Fatihah.
The latecomer (masbuq) says ta'awwudh; the follower does not.
3
Explain the ruku' and its sunnah details
After the surah, the worshiper bows (ruku') with takbir, ensuring tranquility (itmi'nān). The head is level with the back, knees are grasped with spread fingers, and the tasbih 'Subhana Rabbiyal 'Azim' is said at least three times.
Tranquility in ruku' is wajib according to the Hanafi school.
4
Outline the prostration sequence between two sajdahs
From ruku', the worshiper rises and says the appropriate dhikr, then prostrates (sajdah) with takbir, placing knees, then hands, then face (nose and forehead) between the hands. During sajdah, he says the tasbih three times with tranquility, keeps his belly away from his thighs and arms from armpits (unless crowded), and directs fingers and toes toward the qibla. After the first sajdah, he sits with tranquility between the two prostrations, hands on thighs, then performs the second sajdah similarly.
Women are to keep their bodies compact—belly against thighs and arms against sides.
5
Describe the transition to the second rak'ah
After the second sajdah, the worshiper stands up for the next rak'ah with takbir, without leaning on the ground and without any sitting of rest. The second rak'ah is identical to the first except that thanā' and ta'awwudh are not repeated.
This omission is a key mnemonic: thanā' and ta'awwudh are only once in the prayer.

ويَستفتح كلُّ مُصَلِّ.

On how to construct the prayer: 1. Opening: he says 'Subhanaka Allahumma wa bihamdika, wa tabaraka ismuka, wa ta'ala jadduka, wa la ilaha ghayruka.' And every praying person opens with this. 6. Then he seeks refuge quietly for recitation; the latecomer (masbuq) says it, not the follower (muqtadi), and it is delayed behind the takbirs of the two Eids. 7. Then he says the basmalah quietly, and says it in every rak'ah before al-Fatihah only. 8. Then he recites al-Fatihah, and the imam and follower say 'Amin' quietly. 9. Then he recites a surah or three verses. 10. Then he says takbir while bowing: a. with tranquility; b. making his head level with his backside; c. grasping his knees with his hands; d. spreading his fingers; e. and he glorifies (says Subhana Rabbiyal 'Azim) three times, and that is the minimum. 11. Then he raises his head and stands with tranquility saying: 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah, Rabbana laka al-hamd' if he is imam or alone; the follower suffices with the tahmid. 12. Then he says takbir while lowering for prostration. 13. Then he places: a. his knees; b. then his hands; c. then his face between his palms; d. and prostrates on his nose and forehead; e. glorifies three times, and that is the minimum, with tranquility; f. and he keeps his belly away from his thighs and his upper arms from his armpits unless in crowding; g. directing the fingers of his hands and his feet towards the qibla. The woman lowers and sticks her belly to her thighs. 14. Then he raises his head saying takbir, and sits between the two prostrations, placing his hands on his thighs, with tranquility. 15. Then: a. he says takbir; b. prostrates with tranquility; c. glorifies three times; d. keeps his belly away from his thighs; e. and exposes his upper arms. 16. Then he raises his head saying takbir for standing up, without leaning on the ground with his hands, and without sitting. [The second rak'ah:] And the second rak'ah is like the first, except he does not recite the opening supplication (thanā') nor seek refuge (ta'awwudh).
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Nur al-Idah detailing the prescribed actions (af'al) of the prayer (salah) according to the Hanafi school. It begins with the opening supplication (thanā') which is sunnah for every person praying, whether imam, follower, or alone. The thanā' is recited after the takbirat al-ihram. Next, the ta'awwudh (seeking refuge from Shaytan) is recited quietly before the Fatihah. A key distinction: the masbuq (latecomer) says the ta'awwudh, but the muqtadi (follower) does not, because the follower's recitation is replaced by listening to the imam. The ta'awwudh is delayed behind the takbirs of the two Eids (meaning it is not said between those extra takbirs). Then the basmalah is said quietly before the Fatihah in every rak'ah. The imam and follower both say 'Amin' quietly after the Fatihah. After the Fatihah, one recites a surah or three short verses. Then the ruku' (bowing) is performed with tranquility (itmi'nān), which is wajib, leveling the head with the back, grasping the knees with hands spread, and saying the tasbih three times (the minimum recommended). After raising from ruku', one says the appropriate dhikr: the imam and lone worshiper say 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah, Rabbana laka al-hamd', while the follower only says the tahmid part. The prostration (sajdah) is done by placing knees first, then hands, then face (nose and forehead) between the hands, with tranquility, saying tasbih three times, keeping the belly away from the thighs and arms from armpits unless crowded, and directing the fingertips and toes toward the qibla. For women, they are instructed to lower themselves and keep the belly close to the thighs (i.e., to be more compact). After each prostration, one sits between the two prostrations with tranquility, hands on thighs, then performs the second prostration similarly. After the second prostration, one stands up for the next rak'ah without leaning on the hands and without sitting (i.e., without a sitting of rest). The second rak'ah is identical to the first except that thanā' and ta'awwudh are not repeated. All these actions and their details are based on the practice of the Prophet ﷺ as transmitted by the Hanafi imams.

Key Terms
ثناء (thanā'): The opening supplication recited after takbirat al-ihram: 'Subhanaka Allahumma...'
تعوذ (ta'awwudh): Seeking refuge with Allah from Shaytan, recited quietly before the Fatihah in the first rak'ah only.
تسمية (tasmiyah): Reciting 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' quietly before the Fatihah in every rak'ah.
مطمئناً (mutma'innan): Performing an action with tranquility and stillness, meaning each body part settles in its position before moving.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The prayer's actions are meticulously ordered; thanā' and ta'awwudh are only in the first rak'ah, and tranquility is wajib for the key postures.

The Sunnah Actions of Prostration
Describes the sunnah actions of prostration and the sitting between.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah actions of prostration and explain the difference between sunnah and wajib in this context.

Core Idea
Prostration has many sunnah acts, but tranquility (itminan) is wajib for the pillar to be valid.

The prostration (sujud) is a pillar of prayer, but the manner in which it is performed includes recommended (sunnah) actions that perfect it. This lesson focuses on the detailed descriptions from the Hanafi matn, clarifying what is obligatory and what is merely commendable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the body parts involved
List the body parts specified: face (placed between palms), nose, forehead, fingers, toes. Note the direction (qibla).
The nose and forehead must touch the ground; the face between palms is a sunnah placement.
2
Distinguish sunnah from wajib
Locate the phrase 'مطمئناً' (with tranquility) and 'مسحاً ثلاثاً' (tasbih three times). Recognize that tranquility is wajib (necessary), while tasbih is sunnah.
The minimum tasbih is three times; more is recommended.
3
Describe the body posture
Explain the sunnah of keeping the belly away from thighs and arms away from sides, except when crowded. Note the difference for women.
Women are instructed to lower themselves and keep the belly close to thighs for modesty.
4
Understand the ruling category
Classify each action as rukn, wajib, sunnah, or mubah based on Hanafi fiqh as presented in the matn.
The prostration itself is a rukn; tranquility is wajib; the other actions are sunnah.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement detailing the sunnah manner of prostration and the sitting between prostrations in prayer. It begins by describing the placement of the face between the palms during prostration, then emphasizes that the prostration is on the nose and forehead. The minimum tasbih (glorification) in prostration is three times, and all actions must be performed with tranquility (itminan). The person should keep his belly away from his thighs and his upper arms away from his flanks, but if crowded, this is relaxed. The feet and toes should point toward the qibla. For women, the description differs: they should lower themselves and keep their belly against their thighs, which is more modest. After the prostration, one sits between the two prostrations with hands on thighs, also with tranquility. Then for the second prostration, the same steps are repeated: takbir, tasbih (three times), exposing the arms, prostration with tranquility, and keeping the belly away. Then one stands directly without support or sitting. The second rak'ah is like the first except that the thana' (opening supplication) and ta'awwudh (seeking refuge) are not recited, since they are only said at the beginning of the first rak'ah.

Key Terms
مطمئناً (mutma'innan): Performing the action with calmness and stillness, without haste, so that each limb settles.
مسحاً ثلاثاً (tasbīḥan thalāthan): Uttering subhana rabbi al-a'la three times, which is the minimum.

The Sitting Between Prostrations and Standing Up
Describes the sunnah actions of prostration and the sitting between.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the sunnah manner of the sitting between prostrations and the standing up, and note the exception for the second rak'ah.

Core Idea
The sitting between prostrations is a wajib action, and standing without support is sunnah, emphasizing tranquility throughout.

After the first prostration, the worshiper sits briefly before the second. This lesson explains the posture, the repetition of the same sunnah actions in the second prostration, and the distinctive way of standing for the next rak'ah. It also notes that the second rak'ah repeats the first except for the opening supplication and seeking refuge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sitting posture
Locate the description of the sitting between the two prostrations: raising the head with takbir, sitting with hands on thighs, with tranquility.
The sitting is brief; the hands are placed on the thighs.
2
Recognize the repetition in the second prostration
Notice that the second prostration is performed in the same manner as the first, including takbir, tasbih, exposing arms, tranquility, and belly away from thighs.
The text enumerates the same steps for the second sujud as the first.
3
Understand the standing up
Describe how to stand: raise the head with takbir, without supporting on the ground with hands, and without sitting (i.e., no pause).
This is a sunnah manner of rising.
4
Note the difference in the second rak'ah
The second rak'ah is like the first, except thana' (opening supplication) and ta'awwudh (seeking refuge) are omitted.
Those are only recited in the first rak'ah.

D) It is mustahabb, and omitting it is makruh.

Key Takeaway
The sitting between prostrations is wajib, the sujud actions are sunnah, and standing without support is also sunnah; the second rak'ah omits the opening supplications.

Raising Hands During Prayer: Opening Takbir, Qunut, and Eid
Listing the first three occasions for raf' al-yadayn in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three occasions within prayer (opening takbir, qunut takbir, and Eid takbirat) where raising the hands is sunnah in the Hanafi madhhab, and state the general rule that it is not sunnah elsewhere.

Core Idea
In Hanafi, raise hands only at the opening takbir, not when bowing or rising.

Many students assume raising hands is sunnah during every takbir of the prayer. The Hanafi madhhab limits this to just three specific moments within the prayer itself: the opening takbir, the takbir for qunut in witr, and the extra takbirat of Eid. This lesson anchors those three occasions and clarifies the general rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Note the opening statement: 'Raising the hands is not sunnah except...' This means that by default, raf' al-yadayn is not a sunnah act; it is only recommended in the exceptions listed.
2
Extract the prayer-related exceptions
From the list, pick out items 1, 2, and 3: opening takbir of every prayer, takbir for qunut in witr, and the additional takbirat of Eid prayers.
These are the three occasions that occur during salah.
3
Memorize the occasions
Commit these three to memory: 1) takbirat al-ihram, 2) takbir al-qunut in witr, 3) takbirat al-zawa'id in Eid. Understand that they are distinct from the other takbirat of the prayer.
Use the numbering in the source for quick recall.
4
Apply the negative implication
Recognize what is NOT included: raising hands is not sunnah when going into ruku', rising from ruku', during sujud, or during the ordinary takbir of transition. This is the key difference from other schools.

Raising Hands During Hajj, Umrah, and Du'a
Listing the remaining nine occasions for raf' al-yadayn outside prayer.

By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the nine occasions outside of prayer (seeing Kaaba, touching Black Stone, Safa, Marwa, Arafah, Muzdalifah, after first and middle Jamrah, and du'a after tasbih) where raising the hands is sunnah in the Hanafi madhhab.

Core Idea
Outside prayer, raise hands at nine key moments: seeing Kaaba, during Hajj/Umrah rites, and after tasbih.

The Hanafi madhhab raises hands not only in prayer but also at several moments during Hajj, Umrah, and even after completing the tasbih following any prayer. This lesson covers these nine occasions, many of which are specific to pilgrimage or supplication.

The First Sitting: Posture and Recitation
Describes sunnah postures and wajib recitation for first sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the sunnah postures for men and women and the wajib recitation for the first sitting (qu'ud awwal) in the Hanafi madhhab.

Core Idea
Men sit iftirāsh, women sit tawarruk; the tashahhud is wājib, not sunnah.

After completing the two prostrations of the second rak'ah, the worshipper enters the first sitting. This passage from Nur al-Idah details the prescribed postures and the required recitation. Understanding these elements ensures your prayer follows the sunnah and that you can properly compensate if any wājib component is omitted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time of the first sitting
The first sitting occurs immediately after the two prostrations of the second rak'ah. This is the key contextual marker.
There is no first sitting in the two-rak'ah Fajr prayer; it becomes the final sitting.
2
Distinguish the postures for men and women
Men perform iftirāsh: the left foot is spread horizontally and sat upon, the right foot is vertical with toes pointing toward the qibla. Women perform tawarruk: sitting on the left hip with both feet brought out to the right side.
The term iftirāsh literally means 'spreading out', while tawarruk refers to resting on the hip.
3
Note the hand placement
Both hands are placed on the thighs with the fingers spread open. This is part of the sunnah posture.
Some later Hanafi texts specify that the fingers should be spread, not clenched.
4
Recognize the required recitation
The tashahhud according to the narration of Ibn Mas'ud (radiyallahu anhu) is recited. This version is the preferred one in the Hanafi school.
The tashahhud begins: 'At-tahiyyatu lillahi...' and includes the shahada.
5
Understand the legal status of each element
The postures (iftirāsh/tawarruk and hand placement) are sunnah – omitting them does not invalidate the prayer nor require compensation. The recitation of tashahhud is wājib – if omitted, the prayer is valid but requires sajdah sahw (prostration of forgetfulness).
This distinction between sunnah and wājib is fundamental in Hanafi fiqh for knowing the consequence of omission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[القعود الأول :] وإذا فرغ من سجدتي الركعة الثانية: ۱ - افترش رجله اليسرى، وجلس عليها. ٢ - ونصب يمناه ، ووجه أصابعها نحو القبلة. ووضع يديه على فخذيه، وبَسَطَ أصابعه. والمرأة تتورك. وقرأ تشهد ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه.
[The first sitting: ] And when he finishes the two prostrations of the second rak'ah: 1 - He spreads his left foot and sits on it. 2 - And he erects his right foot and points its toes towards the qibla. And he places his hands on his thighs and spreads his fingers. And a woman sits with tawarruk (sitting on the left hip with feet out to the right). And he recites the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him).
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement detailing the sunnah posture for the first sitting (al-qu'ud al-awwal) in prayer, which occurs after the two prostrations of the second rak'ah. The person is to spread (iftrash) his left foot horizontally and sit on it, while erecting his right foot vertically with toes pointing towards the qibla. His hands are placed on his thighs with fingers spread. This is the iftrash position. For women, the recommended sitting is tawarruk, where she sits with her left hip and both feet protrude to the right side, for modesty. During this sitting, one recites the tashahhud (attestation of faith) as transmitted by 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him), which is the preferred version in the Hanafi school. The tashahhud is a wajib (obligatory) part of the prayer according to Hanafis; however, this passage does not discuss its legal status but simply states the recitation.

Key Terms
افترش رجله اليسرى (iftarasha rijlahu al-yusrā): To spread the left foot horizontally and sit on it, with the top of the foot on the ground.
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The recitation of the attestation of faith: 'At-tahiyyatu lillahi...' etc.
تت ورك (tatawarrak): For a woman to sit on her left hip with both feet brought out to the right side, a more modest posture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He sits on both heels (iq'ā').
B) He spreads his left foot and sits on it, erects his right foot.
C) He sits tawarruk like a woman.
D) He kneels with both feet upright.

Key Takeaway
The first sitting requires the iftirāsh posture for men, tawarruk for women, and the wājib recitation of the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud.

The First Sitting (Qa'dah Awwal): Posture, Tashahhud, and Pointing
Describes the posture, tashahhud recitation, and finger movement in the first sitting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the recommended actions in the first sitting of prayer and identify which are sunnah vs. wajib.

Core Idea
In the first sitting, the tashahhud is wajib; the posture and finger movement are sunnah, and no extra supplications are added.

The first sitting (qa'dah awwal) is a wajib element of the prayer, but many of its details are sunnah. This lesson breaks down the exact posture for men and women, the recitation of the tashahhud according to the Hanafi school, and the sunnah of pointing with the index finger. Understanding these distinctions helps ensure your prayer follows the established practice of the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the first sitting itself is wajib; its omission requires sajdah sahw. The posture and finger movement are sunnah.
2
State the sunnah posture for men and women
For men: iftirash (sitting on left foot, right foot upright). For women: tawarruk (sitting on left buttock with legs out to the right).
3
Recite the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud
Recite the testimony as transmitted from Ibn Mas'ud. During the shahadah, point with the index finger: raise at 'la ilaha', lower at 'illallah'.
This is the mufta bihi version in the Hanafi school.
4
Know what not to add
In the first sitting, do not recite any salawat or additional supplications beyond the tashahhud; those are reserved for the final sitting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا فرغ من سجدتي الركعة الثانية: ۱ - افترش رجله اليسرى، وجلس عليها. ٢ - ونصب يمناه ، ووجه أصابعها نحو القبلة. ووضع يديه على فخذيه، وبَسَطَ أصابعه. والمرأة تتورك. وقرأ تشهد ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه. وأشار بالمسبحة في الشهادة يرفعها عند النفي، ويضعها عند الإثبات ولا يزيد على التشهد في القعود الأول، وهو: التحيات لله، والصلوات والطيبات السلام عليك أيها النبي ورحمة الله وبركاته، السلام علينا وعلى عباد الله الصالحين، أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله، وأشهد أن محمداً عبده ورسوله.
And when he finishes the two prostrations of the second rak'ah: 1- he spreads his left foot and sits on it. 2- he erects his right foot and directs its toes towards the qiblah. And he places his hands on his thighs and spreads his fingers. And the woman sits tawarruk. And he recites the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with him. And he points with the index finger during the testimony: he raises it at the negation, and lowers it at the affirmation. And he does not add beyond the tashahhud in the first sitting. And it is: 'Attahiyyatu lillahi, wassalawaatu wattayyibaatu, assalamu 'alayka ayyuha annabiyyu warahmatullahi wabarakatuh, assalamu 'alayna wa 'ala 'ibadillahi assalihin, ashhadu an la ilaha illallah, wa ashhadu anna Muhammadan 'abduhu wa rasuluh.'
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement detailing the method of the first sitting (qa'dah awwal) in prayer. For a man, it is sunnah to sit muftarishan: sitting on the left foot with the right foot upright, toes directed to the qiblah, hands on thighs with fingers spread. For a woman, the sunnah is to sit tawarruk: sitting on the left buttock with both legs out to the right. The recitation is the tashahhud narrated by Ibn Mas'ud. During the testimony phrase, the sunnah is to point with the index finger: raising it when saying 'la ilaha' (negation) and lowering it when saying 'illallah' (affirmation). Crucially, in the first sitting, one does not add any other supplications (like salawat or dua) beyond the tashahhud; that is reserved for the final sitting.

Key Terms
افتراش (iftirash): Sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect, a recommended posture for men in the first sitting.
تورك (tawarruk): Sitting on the left buttock with both legs out to the right, recommended for women in both sittings and for men in the last sitting.
تشهد (tashahhud): The testimony of faith recited in the sitting, specifically the version of Ibn Mas'ud in Hanafi school.
مسبحة (musabbihah): The index finger, used for pointing during the tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Completion of Prayer (Tatimmah as-Salah)
Lists the remaining actions after the first two rak'ahs: recitation, tashahhud, salawat, du'a, and taslim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prescribed sequence of actions after the first two rak'ahs and identify which actions are wajib and which are sunnah in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The prayer concludes with a specific sequence: Fatiha, tashahhud, salawat, du'a, then taslim — each with its own ruling.

This passage from Nur al-Idah presents a clear, step-by-step guide for completing the prayer after the first two rak'ahs. It is a key memorization anchor for the Hanafi method, detailing the order and legal status of each action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
Recognize that this passage describes the 'completion' (tatimmah) of the prayer, applicable to prayers of three or four rak'ahs after the first two.
The sequence is a defined set of wajib and sunnah actions.
2
List the Components in Order
Note the numbered steps: 1) reciting al-Fatiha in the remaining rak'ahs, 2) sitting and reciting tashahhud, 3) sending salawat upon the Prophet, 4) making du'a, 5) saying taslim to the right and left.
This order must be followed; skipping a wajib component requires sajdah sahwah.
3
Determine the Hukm for Each
Classify each action: reciting al-Fatiha in each remaining rak'ah is wajib; tashahhud is wajib; salawat and du'a are sunnah; taslim (twice) is wajib.
Hanafi usul: wajib means required but proven by zanni dalil; omission demands compensation (sajdah sahwah) but does not invalidate the prayer.
4
Note the Intention in Taslim
The taslim is said with the intention of greeting those present (angels and fellow worshippers), as clarified in earlier lessons on intention.
Intention is a condition for the validity of taslim in the Hanafi school.
5
Recognize Application
This sequence applies to all four-rak'ah prayers (Dhuhr, Asr, Isha) and the three-rak'ah Maghrib, after the first two rak'ahs.
For two-rak'ah prayers (Fajr), the entire prayer is the 'first two' and the completion is immediate after tashahhud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الصلاة :
١ - وقرأ الفاتحة فيما بعد الأُولين.
٢- ثم جلس، وقرأ التشهد.
٣- ثم صلى على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم.
٤ـ ثم دعا بما يُشبه القرآن، والسُّنَّةَ.
ه ثم سلم يميناً ويساراً، فيقول: السلام عليكم ورحمة الله»، ناوياً من معه، كما تقدم.
Completion of the prayer: 1- He recites al-Fatiha in what is after the first two (rak'ahs). 2- Then he sits and recites the tashahhud. 3- Then he sends blessings upon the Prophet, peace be upon him. 4- Then he supplicates with what resembles the Quran and the Sunnah. 5- Then he says the taslim to the right and left, saying 'Assalamu alaykum wa rahmatullah', intending those with him, as earlier.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement describing the sequence of actions for completing the prayer after the first two rak'ahs (applicable to four-rak'ah prayers). The worshipper recites Surah al-Fatiha in the third and fourth rak'ahs, then sits for the final tashahhud. After reciting the tashahhud, he sends salawat (blessings) upon the Prophet, and then makes a du'a that is appropriate (resembling Quranic phrases and authentic supplications from the Sunnah). Finally, he performs taslim: turning his head to the right and then left, saying 'Assalamu alaykum wa rahmatullah' each time, intending by the greeting those present (angels and fellow worshippers). This is the standard Hanafi conclusion of prayer.

Key Terms
تتمة الصلاة (tatimmah as-salah): The concluding part of the prayer after the first two rak'ahs, encompassing the remaining rak'ahs, final sitting, and taslim.
التسليم (at-taslim): The greeting of peace that concludes the prayer, performed by turning the head to the right and left.
دعا بما يشبه القرآن والسنة (da'aa bima yushbihu al-Quran was-Sunnah): To supplicate with words that are similar to the Quran and authentic hadith, meaning permissible and appropriate du'as.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Taslim, then du'a
B) Recite Fatiha, then sit, then tashahhud, then salawat, then du'a, then taslim
C) Du'a, then taslim, then tashahhud
D) Tashahhud, then Fatiha, then taslim

Key Takeaway
The completion of the prayer after the first two rak'ahs follows a specific order: Fatiha, tashahhud, salawat, du'a, and taslim; reciting Fatiha in each remaining rak'ah, tashahhud, and taslim are wajib, while salawat and du'a are sunnah.

The Hanafi Method for Tashahhud and Completing Salah
Describes the actions of tashahhud and concluding salat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the actions of tashahhud and the completion of salah according to the Hanafi school, and distinguish the sunnah nature of these acts from obligatory elements.

Core Idea
The tashahhud finger movement and sequence of completing salah are sunnah acts that perfect the prayer.

This passage from Nur al-Idah outlines the practical steps for the tashahhud and concluding the prayer according to the Hanafi school. It emphasizes the recommended (sunnah) nature of these actions and provides the precise wording. Understanding these details helps ensure the prayer is performed with the completeness prescribed by the Hanafi tradition, while also avoiding the common mistake of treating these sunnah acts as obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions during tashahhud
Read the first part of the passage and note the specific action with the index finger: raising it at negation (لا إله) and lowering it at affirmation (إلا الله). This is a sunnah act.
The finger movement is unique to Hanafi fiqh; other schools may prescribe different gestures.
2
Memorize the tashahhud wording
The passage gives the exact wording of the tashahhud as used by the Hanafi school. Read it carefully and commit it to memory.
This wording includes 'السلام عليك أيها النبي' which is also used by most other schools.
3
List the steps after the first two rak'ahs
The passage enumerates the steps: reciting al-Fatiha in the remaining rak'ahs, sitting for tashahhud, sending salawat upon the Prophet, making a du'a, and saying salam to both sides.
These steps apply to prayers of three or four rak'ahs (e.g., Maghrib, Isha, Zuhr, Asr).
4
Note the sunnah status
All actions described in this passage are sunnah (recommended), not wajib or fard. Omitting them does not invalidate the prayer, but doing them is rewarded and completes the perfection of salah.
The passage does not mention any ruling of invalidity; it is simply describing the recommended manner.
5
Apply in practice
Use this description to review your own salah. Check that you are following the Hanafi sunnah in tashahhud and completion, without falling into the misconception that these acts are obligatory.
If in a congregation led by a non-Hanafi imam, it is permissible to follow his lead, but one should be aware of differences.

The manner of performing the prayer: He points with the index finger in the testimony - raising it at negation and lowering it at affirmation. He does not add to the tashahhud in the first sitting. It is: 'All greetings, prayers, and good deeds are for Allah. Peace be upon you, O Prophet, and the mercy of Allah and His blessings. Peace be upon us and upon the righteous servants of Allah. I bear witness that there is no deity but Allah, and I bear witness that Muhammad is His servant and messenger.' [Completion of the prayer:] 1. He recites al-Fatiha in the remaining rak'ahs. 2. Then he sits and recites the tashahhud. 3. Then he sends salawat upon the Prophet, peace be upon him. 4. Then he makes supplication that resembles the Quran and Sunnah. 5. Then he says salam to the right and left, saying 'Peace be upon you and the mercy of Allah,' intending those with him, as previously mentioned.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement detailing the manner of tashahhud and the completion of the salah according to the Hanafi school. It instructs the worshipper to raise the index finger at the negation of lordship for others (at 'la ilaha') and lower it at the affirmation of Allah's divinity (at 'illa Allah'). The prescribed tashahhud wording is given, which is the standard one used in the Hanafi school. Then, the steps for completing the prayer after the first two rak'ahs are listed: reciting al-Fatiha in the remaining rak'ahs, sitting for tashahhud, sending salawat upon the Prophet, making a du'a that is similar to Quranic or prophetic supplications, and finally saying salam to both sides. This is a concise guide for the practical performance of the prayer.

Key Terms
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The testimony recited while sitting in prayer, beginning with 'التَّحِيَّاتُ للهِ'.
المسبحة (al-musabbihah): The index finger, used for pointing during tashahhud.

Conditions of Imamate and Ruling on Congregational Prayer
States the virtue and conditions of imamate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six conditions for valid imamate and explain that congregational prayer is sunnah mu'akkadah.

Core Idea
Congregational prayer is emphatic sunnah; imamate requires Islam, puberty, sanity, male, recitation ability, and freedom from excuses.

This lesson establishes the virtue of imamate and the legal status of congregational prayer. The text then lists six conditions that must be met for a person to lead the prayer validly. Understanding these conditions is essential for anyone who may lead prayers or choose an imam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the virtue of imamate
Note that the text states imamate is better (afdal) than the adhan. The footnote adds that it is preferable for the imam to be the one who calls the adhan.
2
Determine the ruling on congregational prayer
Recognize that congregational prayer is sunnah mu'akkadah for free men who are able and have no excuse. The footnote explains that if the people of a city abandon it without excuse, they are commanded to perform it, and if they refuse, they are fought.
This is not fard 'ayn or fard kifayah in Hanafi school.
3
List the six conditions for valid imamate
The text enumerates six conditions: Islam, puberty, sanity, male, ability to recite correctly, and freedom from excuses like chronic nosebleed, lisping, stuttering, or missing a condition like purity or covering 'awrah.
Memorize these conditions in order.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on recitation
The footnote clarifies that Imam Abu Hanifa requires the imam to know at least one verse, while the two companions (Sahibayn) require three verses. The imamate of an illiterate person is invalid.
This difference affects practical application; mufta bihi is the more precautionary view of Sahibayn.

(۳) أي بحفظ ما تصح به الصلاة، وهو آية، على قول الإمام، وثلاث آيات، على قول الصاحبين، فلا تصح إمامة الأمي. ينظر طحطاوي . ص ۲۳۳
Chapter on Imamate: 1. It is superior to the adhan. 2. Congregational prayer is an emphatic sunnah for free men who are able to perform it without an excuse. [Conditions for its validity:] Conditions for the validity of imamate for healthy men are six things: 1. Islam, 2. Puberty, 3. Sanity, 4. Male, 5. Recitation (ability to recite correctly), 6. Freedom from excuses such as nosebleed, lisping, stammering, lisping (lithgh), and missing a condition like purity or covering the nakedness. Footnote 1: It is better that the imam be the one who calls the adhan, as Imam Abu Hanifa did. (Maraqi). Footnote 2: Because it is waived by an excuse, and if the people of a city abandon it without excuse, they are ordered to perform it, otherwise they are fought for it because it is from the symbols of Islam. (Imdad al-Fattah 331, see Ibn Abidin 3/500). Footnote 3: That is, memorizing what makes the prayer valid, which is one verse according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and three verses according to the two companions. So the imamate of one who cannot recite properly is not valid. See al-Tahtawi p. 233.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement starting the chapter on imamate. It first states that imamate (leading prayer) is better than calling the adhan. The footnote clarifies it is preferable for the imam to be the same person who calls adhan, as was the practice of Imam Abu Hanifa. Then it declares that congregational prayer is an emphatic sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah) for free men who are able and have no excuse. The footnote explains the ruling: if the people of a city abandon it without excuse, they are commanded to perform it, or else they are fought, because it is a symbol of Islam. Finally, it lists six conditions for the validity of imamate for healthy men: Islam, puberty, sanity, male, correct recitation, and freedom from excuses like chronic nosebleed, lisping, stuttering, or missing a condition like purity or covering the 'awrah. The footnote on recitation delineates the Hanafi intra-school difference: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the imam must know at least one verse that makes prayer valid, while according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he must know three verses. The imamate of an illiterate person (who cannot recite properly) is not valid.

Key Terms
الإمامة (al-imamah): Leading the congregational prayer; the position of the imam.
صلاة الجماعة (salat al-jama'ah): Prayer performed in congregation led by an imam.
الأمي (al-ummiyy): One who cannot recite properly what is necessary for prayer (one verse or three verses depending on the view).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Conditions for Valid Following (Iqtida) in Prayer
Lists fourteen conditions for valid iqtida

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the fourteen conditions for valid iqtida and apply them to determine whether a given congregational prayer is valid.

Core Idea
Valid following requires intention, compatibility, and proper arrangement – without them, the prayer may be invalid.

This passage from Nur al-Idah enumerates the key conditions that must be met for a follower (muqtadi) to validly pray behind an imam. These conditions cover the follower's intention, the imam's intention when women follow, spatial positioning, identity of the prayer, and barriers. Memorizing these conditions is foundational to understanding congregational prayer in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Follower's intention of following
The follower must intend to follow the imam at the moment of his opening takbir. This intention is a condition for the validity of the iqtida.
Intention must be concurrent with takbir.
2
Imam's intention and gender considerations
A male imam must intend to lead when women are following; otherwise their prayer is invalid. This condition is waived for Jumu'ah and Eid according to one view.
Ikhtilaf exists; Ibn Abidin transmits two views.
3
Spatial positioning of imam and follower
The imam must be slightly ahead, at least by his heel. If they align, the iqtida is invalid according to the text.
Footnotes clarify that alignment makes iqtida invalid.
4
Qualitative parity between imam and follower
The imam must not be in a 'lesser state' than the follower. For example, if the follower is performing fard and the imam is performing nafl, this condition is violated.
Both must be performing the same type of prayer (fard/nafl) or compatible.
5
Identity and timing of the prayer
The imam and follower must be praying the same fard prayer. Additionally, a resident imam cannot lead a traveler in a 4-rak'ah prayer after its time because the traveler's prayer reduces to 2 rak'ahs.
This prevents mixing intentions.
6
Who cannot be an imam: masbuq and barriers
A masbuq (one who missed part of the prayer) cannot lead another. Also, no row of women may separate the imam and the male followers.
A masbuq's prayer is incomplete; a women's row creates a barrier.
