Conditions for the validity of following (iqtida):
And the conditions for the validity of following are fourteen things:
1 - The follower's intention to follow, concurrent with his takbirat al-ihram.
2 - The man's intention of imamah is a condition for the validity of women following him.
And the imam's precedence by his heel over the ma'mum.
4 - That he (the imam) not be in a lesser state than the ma'mum.
5 - That the imam not be praying a different obligatory prayer than that of the ma'mum.
6 - Nor a resident (imam) for a traveler after the time [of the prayer] in four-rak'ah prayers.
7 - Nor a latecomer (masbuq) for someone else.
And that no row of women separate the imam and the ma'mum.
(1) If she does not intend it [imamah], her prayer is invalid; this [condition] is other than for Jumu'ah and the two Eids; it is not required there, as Ibn Abidin (3/585) transmitted from many, and he mentioned another preference that it is required in them as well.
(2) If he aligns with him, the following is valid.
(3) Like his [the follower's] being supererogatory and the imam supererogatory (Maraqi).
(4) Meaning the follower's obligatory prayer.
(5) Meaning the following occurs after the time [of the prayer].
(6) So it would be the following of a performer of obligatory prayer behind a performer of supererogatory prayer with respect to the sitting (qaidah); because the traveler's obligatory prayer does not change after the time.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage lists the conditions for the validity of following an imam (iqtida). The text states there are fourteen conditions in total, but here only seven are enumerated (with numbering issues). The given conditions are: (1) the follower must intend to follow at the time of his opening takbir; (2) a male imam must intend imamah for women to follow him (otherwise women's prayer is invalid, except for Jumu'ah and Eid, where some say intention is not required); (3) the imam should be slightly ahead of the follower (at least by his heel); (4) the imam should not be in an inferior state (e.g., performing nafl while the follower is doing fard, or vice versa, according to a later footnote); (5) the imam must be praying the same fard as the follower; (6) a resident imam cannot lead a traveler in performing a 4-rak'ah prayer after its time (because the traveler's prayer reduces to 2, leading to a mixed intention); (7) a masbuq (one who missed part of the prayer) cannot lead another; and additionally, no row of women should separate the imam and the ma'mum. Footnotes clarify details: impromptu alignment is valid; the condition about state (4) is explained as not differing in type (e.g., both nafl, or one fard and the other nafl depending on timing). This is a dense matn for memorization.

Key Terms
الاقتداء (al-iqtidā’): The act of following the imam in prayer by intending to be led.
المقتدي (al-muqtadī): The person who follows the imam in prayer.
المأموم (al-ma’mūm): Synonym for muqtadi – the one led by the imam.
المسبوق (al-masbūq): One who missed part of the congregational prayer and is completing it later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The validity of following an imam hinges on specific conditions; neglecting any key condition may render the prayer invalid.

Conditions for Valid Following and Permissible Followers in Prayer
Listing fourteen conditions for valid iqtida' and permissible followers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fourteen conditions for valid iqtida' and identify which follower-imam pairings are permissible according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The validity of congregational prayer rests on fourteen specific conditions; any violation renders the follower's prayer invalid.

This passage from Nur al-Idah lays out the prerequisites for following an imam in prayer. Understanding these fourteen conditions is essential for ensuring the correctness of your congregation. The text also lists six cases where following is valid despite apparent differences, clarifying common doubts.

Guided Path
1
List all fourteen conditions sequentially
Read the Arabic list carefully. Group them into categories: intention-related (1-2), state-related (3-7), barrier-related (8-13), and knowledge-related (14).
Pay attention to the footnotes, which explain exceptions and preferred views.
2
Examine each condition's rationale
For each condition, ask: why is this necessary for valid iqtida'? For example, condition 1 ensures the follower's prayer is linked to the imam from the start.
Some conditions prevent confusion or ensure proper communication (e.g., barriers).
3
Identify intra-Hanafi differences
Note the differences mentioned: condition 1 (intention for Jumu'ah/Eid) and condition 14 (whose belief about nullifiers counts). Understand the tarjih.
The mufta bihi is to require intention in all prayers and to consider the imam's belief.
4
Learn the six permissible follower types
Memorize the list: a person with wudu following a mutayammim, etc. Understand the principle: valid despite apparent deficiency because the imam's state is not inferior in the relevant aspects.
These cases show that the conditions do not demand absolute perfection, only that the imam is not in a lesser state.
5
Apply conditions to a scenario
Create a hypothetical congregation and check each condition. For example, a follower on a boat, imam on shore, no direct line of sight but audible: condition 13 applies? Actually condition 13 is about separate ships, but this tests barrier conditions.
Practice ensures you can debug real-world prayer issues.

Chapter of Imamate
Conditions for the Validity of Following:
The conditions for the validity of following are fourteen things:
1. The intention of the follower to follow (the imam) concurrent with his takbirat al-ihram.
2. The intention of the man to be imam is a condition for the validity of women following him.
3. The imam is ahead with his heel relative to the follower.
4. That the imam is not in a lesser state than the follower.
5. That the imam is not praying a different obligatory prayer than his (the follower's).
6. And not (that the imam is) a resident for a traveler after the time (in a four-rak'ah prayer).
7. And not (that the imam is) preceded (in his prayer) by another.
8. And that there is not a row of women separating the imam and the follower.
(1) If he does not intend it, his prayer is invalid, and this is other than Friday and the two 'Eids; it is not required there, as quoted by Ibn 'Abidin (3/585) from many, and he mentioned another preferred view that it is required in them as well.
(2) Even if he aligns with him: his following is valid.
(3) Like his being a supererogatory, and the imam is performing supererogatory. (Maraqi)
(4) i.e., the obligatory prayer of the follower.
(5) i.e., the following is after the time.
(6) So it is following of one performing an obligatory prayer by one performing supererogatory regarding the final sitting; because the traveler's obligatory prayer does not change after the time.
- And not a river in which a boat passes.
10 - And not a road on which a cart passes.
Chapter of Imamate
11 - And not a wall that obscures knowledge of the imam's movements; if it is not obscure due to hearing or seeing: the following is valid, according to the correct view.
His imam.
12 - And that the imam is not riding, and the follower is walking, or riding a different mount.
13 - And that (the follower) is not in a ship while the imam is in another not connected to it.
14 - And that the follower does not know of his imam's condition something that invalidates (wudu) in the follower's opinion, like emission of blood or vomit after which he did not renew his wudu.
[Whom it is valid to follow:] And valid is the following of:
1 - One with wudu by one doing tayammum.
2 - One washing by one wiping.
3 - One standing by one sitting.
4 - One with a hunchback by his like.
5 - One reciting by his like.
6 - One performing supererogatory prayer by one performing obligatory prayer.
(1) Al-Jassas, al-Hindwani, Qari' al-Hidayah, Ibn al-Humam, and a group preferred that the consideration is the opinion of the imam, not the follower, and upon this are later scholars from the major scholars of India, Shaykh Mahmud al-Hasan al-Diyubandi and Muhammad Anwar Shah al-Kashmiri. See Fath al-Qadir 1/381, Ibn 'Abidin 3/539, al-Nahr al-Fa'iq 1/294, Fayd al-Bari 1/352, Tarajim Sittat Fuqaha' al-'Alam al-Islami by Shaykh 'Abd al-Fattah Abu Ghuddah, p. 70, and my annotations on Sadh al-Hamamah by al-Nabulsi.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah presents the conditions for the validity of following an imam in congregational prayer (iqtida). The author lists fourteen conditions concisely. The first condition is that the follower intends to follow the imam at the moment of his own takbirat al-ihram; this intention is required for the prayer to be valid except in Jumu'ah and 'Eid prayers according to the majority, though some later scholars require it there as well. The second condition states that a man must intend to be imam for women to validly follow him; mere alignment does not suffice. The third condition is that the imam should be slightly ahead of the follower, not level. The fourth condition is that the imam must not be in a lesser state than the follower, e.g., the imam performing supererogatory while the follower performs obligatory, and the like. The fifth condition is that the imam must not be praying a different obligatory prayer than the follower; e.g., a follower praying Zuhr cannot follow an imam praying 'Asr. The sixth condition prohibits a resident from leading a traveler after the time in a four-rak'ah prayer, because the traveler's prayer becomes qasr after the time. The seventh condition prohibits the imam from being preceded in his prayer by another, i.e., being a follower himself while also being imam. The eighth condition prevents a row of women from separating the imam and the male follower without a barrier. Conditions 9-11 deal with physical barriers: a river with boats, a road with carts, and a wall that obscures view—if these prevent knowledge of the imam's movements, following is invalid, but if the follower can see or hear, it is valid. Condition 12 prohibits the imam riding while the follower is walking or riding a different animal. Condition 13 prohibits the follower being in a separate ship not connected to the imam's ship. Condition 14 requires that the follower not know of a condition in the imam that invalidates wudu in the follower's own view (e.g., blood or vomit that the imam did not remove). The passage then lists six cases where following is valid despite apparent differences: a person with wudu following one with tayammum, one washing (in wudu) following one wiping, one standing following one sitting, a hunchback following another hunchback, one reciting (aloud) following another like him, and one performing supererogatory following one performing obligatory. The footnote on page 148 indicates a difference of opinion about whose view is considered regarding nullifiers: the preferred position is the imam's view, not the follower's, as supported by later scholars.

Key Terms
الاقتداء (al-iqtida'): Following an imam in congregational prayer, where the follower performs his prayer in conformity with the imam's actions.
المقتدي (al-muqtadi): The person who follows the imam in prayer; the follower.
الإمام (al-imam): The person leading the congregational prayer; he must meet certain conditions for the prayer to be valid.
تحريمته (tahrimatuhu): The takbirat al-ihram of the follower, which initiates the prayer and prohibits actions outside it.
متنفل (mutanaffil): One performing a supererogatory (nafl) prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which condition deals with the follower's intention?
A) Condition 1
B) Condition 3
C) Condition 14
D) Condition 8

Key Takeaway
The fourteen conditions for valid iqtida' ensure that the imam-follower relationship is properly established; any violation invalidates the follower's prayer.

Conditions for Validity of Following the Imam
Lists barriers to valid following and permissible follower types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the barriers to valid following in congregational prayer and identify which differences between imam and follower are permissible.

Core Idea
Following the imam is invalid if a barrier prevents knowledge of his actions or if the imam's state is invalid, but differences in purification, posture, or intention do not break validity.

In this passage from Nur al-Idah, Imam al-Quduri outlines the conditions for the validity of iqtida'. Some conditions are negative (barriers that must be absent), while others are positive (permissible differences). Understanding these rules helps ensure that congregational prayer is sound, especially in practical scenarios like praying behind an imam in a different state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the barriers (items 10-15)
List the six physical or state-based obstacles that invalidate following: river with boat, road with cart, wall obscuring view, imam riding while follower walking, separate unconnected boats, and follower's knowledge of imam's invalidity according to the follower's belief.
The last barrier has a tarjih that the imam's own view matters, not the follower's.
2
Understand the rationale
Each barrier prevents proper conformity (muwafaqa) in action. For example, a river or road causes distance and disconnection; a wall blocks sight; riding vs. walking causes a mismatch in rukn sequence.
3
List permissible differences
Identify the six permissible follower types: wudu behind tayammum, washing behind wiping, standing behind sitting, normal behind hunchback, gesturing behind gesturing, nafl behind fard.
The principle is that as long as the imam's prayer is valid according to his own state, the follower may differ in purification method, posture, or intention.
4
Note the footnote tarjih
The footnote clarifies that the correct view is to consider the imam's state, not the follower's perception, when determining if the imam's prayer is valid. Thus, if the follower mistakenly believes the imam is invalid, the following remains valid.
This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

10- And [there is no validity if there is] a river in which a boat passes.
11- And [no validity if there is] a road on which a cart passes.
12- And [no validity if there is] a wall that prevents knowledge of the imam's movements, but if there is no confusion due to hearing or sight, then following is valid, according to the correct opinion.
13- And [it is a condition] that the imam not be riding while the follower is walking, or riding a different mount.
14- And [it is a condition] that they not be in one boat while the imam is in another not attached to it.

1- One who performs ablution with water to follow one who performs tayammum.
2- One who washes (his limb) to follow one who wipes.
3- One standing to follow one sitting.
4- One following a hunchback.
5- One gesturing to follow another gesturing.
6- One performing a voluntary prayer to follow one performing an obligatory prayer.
(1) Al-Jassas, al-Hinduwani, Qari' al-Hidayah, Ibn al-Humam, and others preferred that the consideration is the imam's view, not the follower's. Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah, a foundational Hanafi matn, lists the conditions that must be absent for following an imam in congregational prayer to be valid. The first group (items 10-15) are barriers: a river with boats, a road with carts, a wall obstructing view, imam riding while follower walking, being in separate unconnected boats, and the follower knowing that the imam's state (like bleeding) invalidates his prayer according to the follower's own belief (this last condition is subject to the footnote's discussion that the correct view is to consider the imam's belief, not the follower's). The second part states who may validly be followed: a person with wudu can follow a mutayammim, one who washes follows one who wipes, a standing person follows a sitting one, a person follows a hunchback, one who gestures follows another gesturer, and one performing nafl follows one performing fard. This establishes that differences in the type of purification, posture, or intention between imam and follower do not invalidate the validity of following, as long as the imam's prayer is valid according to his own state.

Key Terms
الاقتداء (al-iqtidā'): Following the imam in prayer by conforming to his actions.
مقتدي (muqtadī): The person who follows the imam in congregational prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Proper following requires absence of physical barriers and that the imam's prayer is valid in his own state, while differences in purification and posture are permissible.

Imam's Prayer Invalidated: Obligation to Inform Followers
Ruling on imam's duty to inform followers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an imam must inform followers if his prayer becomes invalid, identify the means of informing, and explain the preferred view based on the precedence of commentaries over fatwas.

Core Idea
The imam must inform followers to the extent possible; the relied-upon view makes this wajib.

This passage addresses a scenario where after the prayer it becomes known that the imam's prayer was invalid. While the imam must repeat his own prayer, he also has a duty to inform those who followed him so they can repeat correctly. The Hanafi school, after some internal debate, settles on the obligation to inform, prioritising the clear statements of the commentaries over later fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The text states: 'the imam is obligated to inform the people to repeat their prayer to the extent possible'. This is the primary ruling.
2
Determine the means of informing
Footnote (1) explains that informing can be by tongue, writing, messenger, etc. The imam must do whatever is possible.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Footnote (2) narrates that some earlier sources (al-Dirayah) said no obligation if the followers are unspecified; later fatwas also said no obligation absolutely. However, the author resolves by preferring the commentaries (shurūḥ) which confirm the obligation.
The principle: 'ما في الشروح مقدم على الفتاوى' (what is in the commentaries takes precedence over fatwas).
4
Understand the tarjih mechanism
The relied-upon view (al-mukhtār) is that informing is wajib. This is based on the authority of the commentaries over later fatwas, as cited from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and al-Tahtawi.
This illustrates a key principle in Hanafi usul al-ifta: later fatwas may be overruled by earlier, more authoritative commentaries.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if an imam discovers after the prayer that his prayer was invalid (e.g., due to a nullifier), he must immediately inform the followers by whatever means possible—speaking to them, sending a message, etc.—so they may repeat their prayer.
The benefit about leading women is a separate ruling; it is disliked to lead women alone without a mahram, but not disliked if a mahram is present or in a mosque.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ظهر بطلان صلاة إمامه : أعاد ، ويلزم الإمام إعلام القوم بإعادة صلاتهم بالقدر الممكن (۱) ، في المختار (۳).

(۱) أي بلسانه أو بكتاب أو رسول ونحو هذا الدر المختار ٦٣٢/٣ . (۲) نقل المؤلف في المراقي ص ٢٤٠ عن الدراية أنه لا يلزم الإمام الإعلام إذا كانوا قوماً غير معينين معلومين، ونقل الطحطاوي تصحيح مجمع الفتاوى عـدم الإخبار مطلقاً، ثم قال نقلاً. عن الدر ٦٣٢/٣ : أن ما في الشروح مقدم على الفتاوى، أي يلزم الإخبار، أما ابن عابدين فزاد نقلاً عمن يقول بأنه لا يلزمه الإخبار.
فائدة : جاء في الدر المختار وابن عابدين ٥٥٠/٣ (ط دمشق): تكره إمامة الرجل للنساء في بيت ليس معهنَّ رجل غيره، ولا محرم منه، كأخته أو زوجته أو أمته، أما إذا كان معهن واحد ممن ذكر، أو أمهنَّ في المسجد : فلا يكره. نقلاً. البحر الرائق ٣٧٣/١.
And if the invalidity of his imam's prayer becomes apparent, he repeats, and the imam is obligated to inform the people to repeat their prayer to the extent possible (1), according to the preferred view (3).

(1) That is, by his tongue, or by writing, or by messenger, and the like. From al-Durr al-Mukhtar 3/632. (2) The author in al-Maraqi p.240 narrated from al-Dirayah that the imam is not obligated to inform if they are an unspecified group, and al-Tahtawi narrated the correction of Majma' al-Fatawa that there is no informing absolutely, then he said, quoting from al-Durr 3/632: that what is in the commentaries takes precedence over fatwas, meaning informing is required. As for Ibn 'Abidin, he added, quoting from one who says he is not obligated to inform.
Benefit: It came in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Ibn 'Abidin 3/550 (Damascus edition): It is disliked for a man to lead women in prayer in a house in which there is no other man besides him, nor a mahram of his, like his sister, his wife, or his slave woman. But if there is one of those mentioned with them, or he leads them in the mosque, it is not disliked. Quoted from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 1/373.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where after the prayer, it becomes known that the imam's prayer was invalid (e.g., due to a nullifier that occurred but was unknown at the time). The Hanafi ruling: the imam must repeat his own prayer, and he is obligated to inform the followers so they may also repeat theirs, to the extent possible (verbally, in writing, etc.). There is some difference among later scholars: some said he need not inform if the followers are not specific individuals, but the preferred view (al-mukhtār) from the commentaries (shurūḥ) is that he must inform. The passage then includes a benefit: it is disliked (makrūh) for a man to lead women alone in a house without a mahram, but not disliked if a mahram is present or if it is in the mosque. This is an additional useful ruling for the imam's qualifications.

Key Terms
الإعلام (al-i'lām): The imam's act of informing the followers that his prayer was invalid.
بطلان صلاة (buṭlān ṣalāt): The state of a prayer being invalid due to a nullifier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is not required if the followers are not specifically known.

Key Takeaway
An imam whose prayer becomes invalid must inform his followers to repeat theirs, and this is wajib according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Ruling on Informing Congregation of Imam's Invalid Prayer
Ruling on imam's duty to inform followers of prayer invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the imam informing followers of an invalid prayer and identify the mufta bihi within the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
An imam must inform his followers if his prayer was invalid, to the best of his ability.

This lesson addresses a practical issue in congregational prayer: when it becomes known that the imam's prayer was invalid due to a missing condition or pillar, what are the obligations on the imam and followers? We will examine the ruling, the evidence, and the intra-Hanafi discussion, concluding with the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
When the invalidity of the imam's prayer becomes apparent, the followers must repeat their prayer, and it is wajib (obligatory) upon the imam to inform them to the extent possible.
The text uses 'يلزم' which indicates obligation.
2
Understand the condition
The obligation is conditional on the imam's ability to inform: he must do so by tongue, writing, or messenger.
This is derived from 'بالقدر الممكن' (to the extent possible).
3
Explore the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi sources (al-Diraya, Majma' al-Fatawa) held that if followers are unknown or absolutely, informing is not required. The majority view (al-mukhtar) is that it is required.
The footnote mentions that what is in the commentaries (shuruh) takes precedence over fatwa collections.
4
Recognize the tarjih mechanism
The preferred view is that the imam must inform, as established in the major commentaries like al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Ibn Abidin.
Al-Tahtawi and others affirmed that the commentaries' ruling is decisive.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if an imam discovers his prayer was invalid, he is obligated to alert the congregation as best he can, ensuring they repeat their prayer.
This applies today via announcements, messages, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الإمامة
وإن ظهر بطلان صلاة إمامه : أعاد ، ويلزم الإمام إعلام القوم بإعادة صلاتهم بالقدر الممكن (۱) ، في المختار (۳).

(۱) أي بلسانه أو بكتاب أو رسول ونحو هذا الدر المختار ٦٣٢/٣ . (۲) نقل المؤلف في المراقي ص ٢٤٠ عن الدراية أنه لا يلزم الإمام الإعلام إذا كانوا قوماً غير معينين معلومين، ونقل الطحطاوي تصحيح مجمع الفتاوى عـدم الإخبار مطلقاً، ثم قال نقلاً. عن الدر ٦٣٢/٣ : أن ما في الشروح مقدم على الفتاوى، أي يلزم الإخبار، أما ابن عابدين فزاد نقلاً عمن يقول بأنه لا يلزمه الإخبار.
فائدة : جاء في الدر المختار وابن عابدين ٥٥٠/٣ (ط دمشق): تكره إمامة الرجل للنساء في بيت ليس معهنَّ رجل غيره، ولا محرم منه، كأخته أو زوجته أو أمته، أما إذا كان معهن واحد ممن ذكر، أو أمهنَّ في المسجد : فلا يكره. نقلاً. البحر الرائق ٣٧٣/١.
Chapter of Imamate. If the invalidity of his imam's prayer becomes apparent: he repeats, and it is incumbent upon the imam to inform the people of the repetition of their prayer to the extent possible (1), according to the chosen view (3). (1) i.e., by his tongue or by a letter or a messenger and the like. Al-Durr al-Mukhtar 3/632. (2) The author transmitted in al-Maraqi p. 240 from al-Diraya that it is not incumbent upon the imam to inform if they are a group not specifically known; and al-Tahtawi transmitted the correction of Majma' al-Fatawa that notification is absolutely not required; then he said, quoting from al-Durr 3/632, that what is in the commentaries takes precedence over the fatawa, i.e., notification is required. As for Ibn Abidin, he added a transmission from one who says that notification is not required. Benefit: It is mentioned in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Ibn Abidin 3/550 (Damascus edition): It is disliked for a man to lead women in prayer in a house where there is no other man with them, nor any mahram from him, such as his sister or wife or slave woman. But if there is one of those mentioned with them, or he leads them in the mosque, it is not disliked. Transmitted from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 1/373.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on imamate. It addresses the situation where it becomes known after the prayer that the imam's prayer was invalid (due to a missing condition or pillar). The two key rulings are: (1) The followers must repeat their prayer because their prayer was dependent on the imam's valid prayer. (2) It is obligatory (wajib) upon the imam to inform the people to the best of his ability—by speaking, writing, or sending a messenger. The text notes that this is the chosen view (al-mukhtar). The footnotes reveal an intra-Hanafi dispute: some earlier authorities (like al-Diraya and Majma' al-Fatawa) held that if the followers are unknown, or absolutely, the imam does not have to inform. However, the preferred position according to the major commentaries (shuruh) takes precedence over fatwa collections, thus the imam must inform. The benefit (fa'idah) adds a separate ruling: it is makruh tanzihi for a man to lead women alone in a house with no other man or mahram present; but if a mahram is present or if it is in the mosque, it is not disliked.

Key Terms
بطلان صلاة (buţlān al-şalāh): The legal invalidity of prayer due to missing a condition or integral.
إعلام (i'lām): Informing others, here specifically the followers, about the invalidity of the prayer so they may repeat it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling (hukm) on the imam informing the congregation if it becomes apparent that his prayer was invalid?
A) It is recommended (mandub) but not obligatory.
B) It is obligatory (wajib) upon the imam to the extent possible.
C) It is not required unless the followers are known.
D) He must inform only if asked.

Key Takeaway
An imam must inform his followers if his prayer becomes invalid, and the followers must repeat the prayer; this is wajib upon the imam to the extent possible.

Eighteen Excuses for Missing Congregational Prayer
Listing of 18 valid excuses for omitting congregational prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eighteen valid excuses that exempt a Hanafi from attending congregational prayer and explain that intention secures reward despite a valid excuse.

Core Idea
A valid excuse removes the wajib of jamā'ah; intention to attend still earns the reward.

Congregational prayer (salāt al-jamā‘ah) is wajib for Hanafi men, but this obligation is not absolute. This passage from Nur al-Idah enumerates eighteen excuses that lift the obligation, ranging from natural phenomena (heavy rain, snow) to physical conditions (blindness, paralysis) and practical needs (studying fiqh, caring for a patient). Importantly, the conclusion teaches that if a person intended to attend but was prevented by a valid excuse, he still receives the full reward of congregational prayer. This highlights the Hanafi emphasis on intention (niyyah) in securing divine recompense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying ruling
The passage presupposes that jamā'ah is wajib (obligatory) for men. The eighteen excuses cancel this obligation; without a valid excuse, missing jamā'ah is sinful.
This ruling is explicitly stated in the first footnote: 'This is based on the opinion of the obligation of congregational prayer, and Allah knows best.'
2
Categorize the excuses
Group the eighteen items into logical categories: natural (1-4: rain, snow, fear, darkness), physical (5-13: imprisonment, blindness, paralysis, amputation, illness, crippled, mud, chronic disease, old age), and practical (14-18: studying fiqh, food, travel, patient care, strong wind at night).
Memorization is easier with categories. Note that strong wind (18) is only an excuse at night, not during the day.
3
Understand the reward principle
The concluding sentence states that if one is prevented by a valid excuse but had the intention to attend, he obtains the reward. This is a key Hanafi principle: niyyah can secure thawāb even when the act is not physically performed.
This is based on the Islamic concept that deeds are by intentions (al-a'māl bi-niyyāt).
4
Apply the list to modern scenarios
Consider how each excuse might apply today (e.g., heavy rain = snowstorm, fear = threat of violence, repetition of fiqh = attending a serious study circle). Recognize that the list is illustrative, not exhaustive; analogous situations may be included via qiyas.
Always check if the excuse is genuine and proportionate before omitting jamā'ah.

Source Text

Chapter of Imamate. Section: What excuses attendance of congregation. Attendance of congregation is excused by one of eighteen things: 1. Rain (meaning heavy rain). 2. And snow/hail. 3. And fear. 4. And darkness (meaning severe). 5. And imprisonment. 6. And blindness. 7. And paralysis. 8. And amputation (meaning cutting of hand and foot). 9. And illness. 10. And being crippled (meaning palsy). 11. And mud/mire. 12. And chronic disease. 13. And old age. 14. And repetition of jurisprudence with a congregation that he misses (meaning if he attends congregational prayer, he would miss his study companions). 15. And presence of food that his soul desires. 16. And intention to travel. 17. And taking care of a patient. 18. And strong wind at night, not during the day. And if he is cut off from the congregation due to a valid excuse, and his intention was to attend had it not been for the excuse, he obtains its reward. (5) Meaning disease. (6) Meaning palsy. (7) Meaning if he attends congregational prayer, he would miss his brothers with whom he studies.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah lists eighteen valid excuses that exempt a person from attending congregational prayer (salat al-jama'ah). The underlying assumption, as stated in the first footnote, is that congregational prayer is obligatory (wajib) in the Hanafi school. The list covers a wide range of circumstances: natural phenomena (heavy rain, snow, darkness), physical conditions (blindness, paralysis, amputation, illness, chronic disease, old age, being crippled), necessities (fear, imprisonment, taking care of a patient, desire for food, travel), and scholarly activities (repetition of fiqh with a study group that would be missed). Excuse 18 specifies that strong wind is only an excuse at night, not during the day. The concluding sentence emphasizes that if one is prevented by a valid excuse, but had the intention to attend, he still receives the reward of congregational prayer. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that intention and valid excuse can secure divine reward despite the physical absence.

Key Terms
جماعة (jamā‘ah): The congregational prayer (salāt al-jamā‘ah) considered obligatory in the Hanafi school.
عذر (‘udhr): A valid excuse that exempts one from the obligation of attending congregational prayer.
ثواب (thawāb): Reward from Allah; here, the reward for congregational prayer is granted despite absence if the intention was present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes a wide range of valid excuses that excuse one from the wajib act of congregational prayer, and intention compensates for physical absence.

Reward for Intending Congregation Despite Excuse
States that one who intends congregation but is excused still receives reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding reward for intending congregational prayer despite a valid excuse and distinguish it from the condition of actual attendance.

Core Idea
Sincere intention for congregation, even when prevented by a valid excuse, earns the full reward.

This lesson clarifies a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: the value of intention. Even if a valid excuse prevents you from attending congregational prayer, your sincere intention to attend is rewarded as if you were present. This ruling encourages believers to maintain their connection with the congregation in their hearts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
The act is intending to attend congregational prayer. The condition is that one is prevented by a valid excuse (e.g., illness, rain, fear).
2
State the ruling
The text states that such a person obtains the reward of the congregation.
This is the explicit ruling from the source.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The ruling is based on the principle that intentions are accounted for. A person is rewarded for what he intended to do, even if circumstances prevent him.
This is consistent with the hadith: 'Actions are judged by intentions.'
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some Shafi'i scholars hold that reward for congregation is contingent on actual presence, not mere intention. The Hanafi position is the one presented here.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if one is prevented from [attending] the congregation due to one of its valid excuses, and his intention was to attend it were it not for the excuse: he obtains its reward.

Commentary
This is a ruling regarding the reward for congregational prayer. The Hanafi school teaches that if a person has the sincere intention to attend the congregational prayer but is prevented by a legitimate excuse—such as illness, severe weather, or fear—then he still receives the full reward of the congregation. This is based on the principle that intentions are considered, and Allah rewards according to intention.

Key Terms
الجماعة (al-jamā‘ah): The congregational prayer performed by two or more persons following an imam.
العذر (al-‘udhr): A valid excuse in Islamic law that permits one to miss an obligatory or recommended act without sin.

Key Takeaway

Priority Order for Leading Prayer
Lists the sequence of qualifications for who should lead the prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi priority order for selecting an imam and identify the exceptions that override the order.

Core Idea
Knowledge of prayer rulings, not Quran recitation, is the primary criterion for imam.

When a group gathers for prayer without a designated imam, who should lead? The Hanafi school provides a clear sequence of qualifications, starting with the most knowledgeable in fiqh of salah. However, if the owner of the house, a regular mosque imam, or a ruler is present, they take precedence regardless of other qualifications. This lesson unpacks the list and its underlying wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptions
The list only applies if three categories are absent: owner of the house (sāhib manzil), regular mosque imam (wazīfa), and a person in authority (dhū sulṭān). If any of these is present, they lead unconditionally.
This ensures respect for established authority and avoids conflict.
2
Recognize the first criterion: Knowledge
The first and most important quality is being al-a‘lam, the most knowledgeable in the rulings of prayer (ahkām al-ṣalāh). This includes knowing conditions, pillars, and common issues.
Knowledge of fiqh takes priority over mere recitation skill.
3
List the remaining criteria in order
After knowledge, the next qualities are: best reciter (al-aqra’), most pious (al-awra‘), eldest (al-asan), best character (al-ahsan khuluqan), best appearance, noblest lineage, best voice, and cleanest garment.
Each criterion addresses a different aspect of the imam's role.
4
Understand the rationale
Knowledge ensures correct prayer; recitation beauty enhances congregation's focus; piety inspires trust; age brings maturity; character and appearance reflect dignity; lineage ensures acceptance; voice aids recitation; cleanliness is basic respect.
These are not absolute; they are guidelines for harmony.
5
Note the legal status
This order is mustahabb (recommended), not fard. Deviating from it is not sinful, but following it is meritorious.
The imam chosen should be acceptable to the congregation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأحق بالإمامة
إذا لم يكن بين الحاضرين صاحب منزل، ولا وظيفة، ولا ذو سلطان: فالأعلم أحق بالإمامة.
ثم الأقرأ.
ثم الأورع.
ثم الأسَن.
ثم الأحسن خلقاً.
ثم الأحسن وجهاً.
ثم الأشرف نسباً.
ثم الأحسن صوتاً.

Chapter on who is most entitled to lead the prayer.
If among those present there is neither the owner of the house, nor a regular imam, nor a person in authority: then the most knowledgeable is most entitled to lead.
Then the best reciter.
Then the most pious.
Then the eldest.
Then the best in character.
Then the best in appearance.
Then the noblest in lineage.
Then the best in voice.
Then the cleanest in garment.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah outlines the preferred order for selecting an imam for prayer in a gathering where there is no designated imam (e.g., a regular mosque imam, or a local ruler). The Hanafi school first gives priority to the most knowledgeable person in matters of prayer (al-a‘lam). If equal, then the one who recites the Quran best (al-aqra’). Next, the most pious (al-awra‘), then the eldest (al-asan), then the best in character (al-ahsan khuluqan), then the best in appearance (al-ahsan wajhan), then the noblest in lineage (al-ashraf nasaban), then the one with the best voice (al-ahsan sawtan), and finally the one with the cleanest clothing (al-anzaf thawban). These criteria ensure that the imam is respected and capable of leading the prayer properly.

Key Terms
الأعلم (al-a‘lam): The most knowledgeable in the rulings of prayer (ahkam al-salah).
الأقرأ (al-aqra’): The one who recites the Quran most correctly and beautifully.
الأورع (al-awra‘): The one with the greatest scrupulousness and piety, avoiding doubtful matters.
الأسَن (al-asan): The eldest in age.
صاحب منزل (sāhib manzil): The owner of the house where the prayer is being held.
وظيفة (wazīfa): A regular imam appointed for a mosque (al-imam al-ratib).
ذو سلطان (dhū sulṭān): A person in authority, such as a governor or ruler, who deserves precedence if present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The most knowledgeable.
B) The best reciter.
C) The eldest.
D) The one with the cleanest garment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the most knowledgeable in prayer rulings is the preferred imam unless an owner, regular imam, or ruler is present.

The Hanafi Order of Precedence for Choosing an Imam
Lists the order of precedence for imam selection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi hierarchy of nine criteria for selecting an imam and explain why knowledge is prioritized.

Core Idea
Knowledge first, then recitation, piety, age, character, appearance, lineage, voice, cleanliness.

When no regular imam or authority is present, the congregation must choose an imam. The Hanafi school provides a clear order of preference, with knowledge of fiqh as the primary quality. This ensures the prayer is valid and the community's needs are met.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exceptions
The order only applies when there is no owner of the house, no regular imam (appointed by the endower), and no person of authority. If any of these are present, they take precedence.
The regular imam's right is sacred like the text of the Lawgiver.
2
Memorize the nine criteria in order
From most knowledgeable (a'lam) to cleanest clothing (andhaf thawban). Learn each one sequentially.
Knowledge here means of fiqh, especially conditions of prayer.
3
Know the procedure when all are equal
If all candidates are equal, either draw lots or let the congregation choose. If they disagree, the majority's choice is followed.
Choosing a less qualified person intentionally is blameworthy (asa'u).

Chapter of Imamah. [The Most Entitled to Imamah:] Section on who is most entitled to lead prayer and arrangement of rows. 1- If there is no owner of the house, nor a regular imam (sahib al-wazifah), nor a person of authority among those present: the most knowledgeable is most entitled to lead. 2- Then the best reciter. 3- Then the most pious. 4- Then the oldest. 5- Then the best in character. 6- Then the best in appearance. 7- Then the noblest in lineage. Then the best in voice. 9- Then the cleanest in clothing. (1) meaning the regular imam of the place, who is the deputy of the authority. The original principle is that the regular imam is appointed by the endower; giving precedence to another violates his intent and the condition of the endower, which is like the text of the Lawgiver. See Tahawi. Chapter of Imamah. If they are equal: lots are cast, or the choice is left to the congregation. If they disagree, weight is given to the majority's choice. And if they give precedence to one who is not the most entitled, they have done wrong. [Those whose Imamah is Disliked:] It is disliked (makruh) to lead: 1- A slave. 2- A blind person. 3- A bedouin. 4- An illegitimate child who is ignorant. 5- A transgressor (fasiq). 6- An innovator (mubtadi'). [What is Disliked in Congregation:] 1- Lengthening the prayer. 2- A congregation of naked persons. And women: if they do so (form a congregation), the imam stands in their midst, like the naked. (1) The qualification 'ignorant' includes the bedouin and the illegitimate child; if they are knowledgeable and pious, their imamah is not disliked. See Muraqi.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah presents three key rulings on congregational prayer leadership (imamah). First, when no regular imam or authority is present, the order of precedence for choosing an imam is: (1) most knowledgeable in fard and conditions of prayer, (2) best reciter of Quran, (3) most pious, (4) oldest, (5) best character, (6) best appearance, (7) noblest lineage, (8) best voice, (9) cleanest clothing. If all are equal, either draw lots or let the congregation choose; if they disagree, the majority choice is followed. People sin if they deliberately pass over a more qualified person. Second, six types of people are makruh (disliked) to lead: a slave, a blind person, a bedouin, an illegitimate child who is ignorant, a fasiq (open sinner), and a mubtadi' (innovator in religion). The footnote clarifies that 'ignorant' qualifies both the bedouin and illegitimate child—if they are knowledgeable and pious, it is not disliked. Third, disliked acts in congregation include lengthening prayer unnecessarily, and a congregation of naked persons; if women form a congregation, the imam stands in their middle row, just like for naked persons.

Key Terms
الأحَق بالإمامة (al-ahaqqu bil-imāmah): The person most entitled to lead the prayer based on a hierarchy of qualities.
الأقرأ (al-aqra'): The one who has the best recitation of the Quran.
صاحب الوظيفة (sāhib al-wazīfah): The regular imam appointed by the endower of the mosque.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The most knowledgeable
B) The best reciter
C) The oldest
D) The most pious

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi imam selection prioritizes knowledge; violating the order is blameworthy but does not invalidate the prayer.

Disliked Imams and Disliked Acts in Congregational Prayer
Lists those whose imamah is disliked and disliked acts in congregation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six types of persons whose imamah is disliked (makruh) and explain the exceptional condition of ignorance, and list three disliked acts in congregation.

Core Idea
Six persons are disliked to lead, but if they are knowledgeable and pious, the dislike is removed.

The Hanafi school teaches that while many individuals are valid imams, some are disliked due to social or religious reasons. However, knowledge and piety can override these dislikes. Similarly, certain acts during congregation are disliked to avoid hardship or impropriety.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the six disliked imams
Slave, blind person, bedouin, ignorant illegitimate child, fasiq (open sinner), mubtadi' (innovator). These are makruh tanzih.
The prayer is valid but reward is decreased.
2
Identify the key qualification: ignorance
The text explicitly conditions the bedouin and illegitimate child with 'ignorant'. If they are knowledgeable and pious, there is no dislike.
Knowledge of fiqh removes the dislike.
3
Know the disliked acts in congregation
Lengthening prayer unnecessarily, a congregation of naked persons, and women forming a congregation (where the imam stands in their midst).
These are disliked to avoid hardship or impropriety.

Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah presents three key rulings on congregational prayer leadership (imamah). First, when no regular imam or authority is present, the order of precedence for choosing an imam is: (1) most knowledgeable in fard and conditions of prayer, (2) best reciter of Quran, (3) most pious, (4) oldest, (5) best character, (6) best appearance, (7) noblest lineage, (8) best voice, (9) cleanest clothing. If all are equal, either draw lots or let the congregation choose; if they disagree, the majority choice is followed. People sin if they deliberately pass over a more qualified person. Second, six types of people are makruh (disliked) to lead: a slave, a blind person, a bedouin, an illegitimate child who is ignorant, a fasiq (open sinner), and a mubtadi' (innovator in religion). The footnote clarifies that 'ignorant' qualifies both the bedouin and illegitimate child—if they are knowledgeable and pious, it is not disliked. Third, disliked acts in congregation include lengthening prayer unnecessarily, and a congregation of naked persons; if women form a congregation, the imam stands in their middle row, just like for naked persons.

Key Terms
كره إمامة (kurihat imāmatu): It is disliked (makruh) for these persons to lead; prayer is valid but reward reduced.
جاهل (jāhil): Ignorant of the rulings of prayer and fiqh.
ولد الزنا (walad az-zinā): Illegitimate child (born out of wedlock).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the six types of disliked imams according to this passage?
A) Slave
B) Blind person
C) Wealthy person
D) Innovator (mubtadi')

Key Takeaway
Disliked imams are those with defects; but knowledge and piety can overcome the disadvantage.

Choosing an Imam When Candidates Are Equal
Ruling on how to select an imam when candidates are equal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for selecting an imam when candidates are equal and identify when the congregation is blameworthy.

Core Idea
When imam candidates are equal, choose by lot or congregation consent; majority decides; bypassing the best is blameworthy.

Selecting an imam for salah is a communal responsibility. When multiple candidates are equal in merit, the Hanafi school provides clear guidelines to avoid conflict. This passage from Nur al-Idah outlines the proper procedure and the moral implications of overlooking the most qualified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The ruling applies only when candidates are equal in merit. If one is superior, different rules apply.
Equality is assumed in knowledge, piety, and other relevant qualities.
2
First option: draw lots
Drawing lots (qur'ah) is a legitimate method to decide without favoritism.
It is a sunna method for resolving disputes in Islam.
3
Second option: choice of congregation
The congregation may choose their preferred candidate if they reach consensus.
This preserves communal harmony.
4
If disagreement arises
When the congregation cannot agree, the majority's choice is followed.
Majority decision is binding.
5
Blame for overlooking the best
If the congregation knowingly chooses someone less qualified while a superior candidate exists, they are blameworthy.
The prayer remains valid, but they commit a sin (makruh tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter on imamate (leadership in prayer). It addresses the scenario where multiple candidates for imam are equal in merit. Two methods are given: either draw lots (qur'ah) or leave the choice to the congregation. The drawing of lots is a legitimate method for resolving conflicts in Islamic law. If the congregation disagrees, the opinion of the majority is followed. Finally, if the congregation selects someone who is not the most qualified (al-ula) while someone more qualified is present, they are considered to have done wrong (asa'u), though the prayer behind such an imam is still valid according to the Hanafi school. 'Doing wrong' here implies blameworthiness but does not invalidate the prayer.

Disliked Imams in Congregational Prayer
Listing of six disliked imams with a conditional footnote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six types of imams whose imamate is disliked in the Hanafi school and state the condition that removes the dislike for the Bedouin and illegitimate child.

Core Idea
Six imams are disliked; ignorance triggers the dislike for two of them.

The Hanafi school specifies certain individuals whose leading of congregational prayer is valid but makruh tanzih (disliked). The passage lists six categories: a slave, a blind person, a Bedouin, an illegitimate child (if ignorant), a sinner, and an innovator. However, the dislike for the Bedouin and illegitimate child is conditional upon their ignorance; if they are knowledgeable and pious, their imamate is not disliked. This ruling highlights the importance of the imam's knowledge and moral uprightness, while still recognizing the validity of the prayer led by such individuals.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the six categories
Learn the six types in order: slave, blind, Bedouin, illegitimate child, sinner, innovator. Use the Arabic terms for precision.
The order in the text is 1 to 6.
2
Identify the condition
Note that for the Bedouin and illegitimate child, the dislike is conditional on ignorance. If they are knowledgeable and pious, their imamate is not disliked.
The footnote clarifies this restriction.
3
Understand the hukm level
The imamate of these six is 'makruh tanzih' (disliked but valid). The prayer behind them is valid and does not need to be repeated.
Makruh tanzih means the act is best avoided but not sinful.
4
Differentiate from invalid
Do not confuse 'disliked' with 'invalid'. None of these categories render the prayer invalid; they are just not ideal.
Valid imams: the prayer counts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Those whose imamate is disliked: The imamate of the following is disliked: 1. The slave. 2. The blind. 3. The Bedouin. 4. The illegitimate child who is ignorant. 5. The sinner. 6. The innovator. Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage lists six categories of people whose leading of prayer is disliked (makruh tanzih) in the Hanafi school. The list includes: a slave, a blind person, a Bedouin (an uncivilized desert dweller), an illegitimate child (who is ignorant of religious matters), a sinful person, and a religious innovator. However, the footnote clarifies that the dislike for the Bedouin and the illegitimate child is conditional upon their ignorance. If they are knowledgeable and pious, their imamate is not disliked. This ruling emphasizes the importance of the imam being well-versed in prayer rulings and of upright character. The underlying reason is to ensure the prayer is performed correctly and with proper reverence. The imamate of such individuals is valid but not ideal.

Key Terms
العبد (al-'abd): A male slave; his imamate is disliked due to preoccupation with his master's service
الأعمى (al-a'ma): A blind person; disliked because he cannot see impurity or track rakat
الأعرابي (al-a'rabi): An uncivilized Bedouin; disliked if ignorant of prayer rulings
ولد الزنا (walad al-zina): Child born out of wedlock; disliked if ignorant, not if pious and knowledgeable
الفاسق (al-fasiq): A person who commits major sins openly
المبتدع (al-mubtadi'): A religious innovator who holds beliefs contrary to orthodox Sunnism

Core Idea
Lengthening prayer and naked congregation are disliked; women's congregation requires imam in the middle.

Congregational prayer has etiquettes and rulings. The Hanafi school identifies specific disliked actions to avoid. This passage covers two: excessively prolonging the prayer and forming a congregation entirely of naked people. It also addresses the rare situation of women-only congregation, detailing how the imam should stand to preserve modesty.

Guided Path
1

2
Understand the women's congregation ruling
Note that the passage also addresses women forming a congregation. The imam (a woman) stands in the middle of the row, similar to the arrangement for naked people to minimize exposure.
This ruling is context-specific for modesty; the Hanafi school considers women's congregation makruh tanzih.

Arrangement of Rows in Congregational Prayer
Rulings on placement and row order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on where a single follower stands in congregational prayer and arrange the order of rows by age and gender.

Core Idea
Single follower right of imam; rows: men, boys, hermaphrodites, women.

This lesson covers the Hanafi rules for arranging rows (الصفوف) in congregational prayer. The text specifies the placement of the imam's follower and the correct sequence based on age and gender, ensuring proper organization and modesty. Understanding this ruling helps maintain order and avoid confusion during congregational worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for a single follower
The text states: 'Yaqifu al-wahid 'an yamin al-imam' (the single person stands to the right of the imam). This is a distinct ruling from the case of multiple followers.
For more than one follower, they stand behind the imam.
2
Memorize the row order sequence
The order is: men (رجال), then boys (صبيان), then hermaphrodites (خناثى), then women (نساء). Each group forms rows in that sequence.
The term 'boys' refers to children who have not reached puberty.
3
Understand the responsibility for arranging rows
The footnote clarifies that lining up (الاصطفاف) is the action of the followers, not the imam. However, the imam may instruct them, and there is a difference of opinion on whether this instruction is merely permissible or recommended.
The preferred view is that the imam should order (ينبغي) the followers to straighten their rows.

Commentary
This passage gives the recommended arrangement of rows in congregational prayer. If only one person is following, he stands to the right of the imam. If there are more than one, they stand behind the imam. The order of rows is: first men, then boys (who have not reached puberty), then hermaphrodites (khunatha), and finally women. This order ensures modesty and proper sequence. The footnote clarifies that arranging rows is primarily the responsibility of the followers, not the imam, but the imam may instruct them. There is a difference of opinion on whether this instruction is merely permissible or recommended.

الاصطفاف (al-istifaf): The act of standing in rows; assigned to the followers, not the imam

Rulings on the Follower's Actions After the Imam's Completion
List of juristic rulings for the follower in congregational prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct response of the follower in each of the six scenarios and identify whether following the imam is obligatory, permissible, or prohibited.

Core Idea
The follower must complete his own obligations but never follow the imam into error.

This lesson covers practical scenarios in congregational prayer when the imam acts before or after the follower, or makes a mistake. Understanding these rulings is essential to maintain the validity of your prayer when following an imam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the imam's action
From the six scenarios, determine what the imam did: says salam early, raises head early, adds an extra prostration, stands forgetfully, etc.
2
Classify the action type
Is this a normal completion, a premature act that interrupts the follower's obligations, or a forgetful error?
3
Apply the appropriate ruling
Based on classification: if the imam finishes early, follower completes own obligations; if imam errs by adding acts, follower waits and does not follow.
Key: follower must not follow the imam into error.
4
Check consequences
If the follower acts wrongly (e.g., says salam before imam ties extra rak'ah), prayer becomes invalid. Memorise to avoid invalidating prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يفعله المقتدي بعد فراغ إمامه من واجب وغيره. ١ - لو سلم الإمام قبل فراغ المقتدي من التشهد : يتمه. ٢ - ولو رفع الإمام رأسه قبل تسبيح المقتدي ثلاثاً في الركوع، أو السجود : يتابعه. ٣ - ولو زاد الإمام سجدةً، أو قام بعد القعود الأخير ساهياً: لا يتبعه المؤتم، بل يمكث. ٤ - فإن عاد الإمام قبل تقييده الزائدة بسجدة: سلم. ٥ - وإن قيدها: سلم وحده. ٦ - وإن قام الإمام قبل القعود الأخير ساهياً : انتظره. فإن سلم المقتدي قبل أن يقيد إمامه الزائدة بسجدة: فسد فرضه. وكره سلام المقتدي بعد تشهد الإمام قبل سلامه.
Chapter on what the follower does after his imam finishes from an obligatory act and other than it. 1- If the imam says salam before the follower finishes the tashahhud, he completes it. 2- If the imam raises his head before the follower's three tasbihs in ruku or sujud, he follows him. 3- If the imam adds a prostration or stands after the final sitting forgetfully, the follower does not follow him but remains. 4- If the imam returns before tying the extra (rak'ah) with a prostration, he says salam. 5- And if he ties it, he says salam alone. 6- If the imam stands before the final sitting forgetfully, he waits for him. If the follower says salam before his imam ties the extra with a prostration, his prayer is invalid. And it is disliked for the follower to say salam after the imam's tashahhud before his salam.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This section provides detailed rulings for the follower (ma'mum) when the imam performs actions after the obligatory parts or due to forgetfulness. The key principle is that the follower must maintain following (mutaba'ah) but is excused when the imam acts incorrectly. For example, if the imam says salam before the follower finishes tashahhud, the follower must complete his tashahhud before following. Similarly, if the imam raises his head from ruku or sujud before the follower completes three tasbihs, the follower should stop his tasbih and follow immediately. However, if the imam adds an extra prostration or stands up after the final sitting forgetfully, the follower must not follow; rather, he waits. If the imam then returns (before completing the extra rak'ah), the follower says salam with him. If the imam completes the extra rak'ah, the follower says salam alone. When the imam stands forgetfully before the final sitting, the follower waits for him to return. An important warning: if the follower says salam before the imam completes the extra rak'ah with a prostration (i.e., before the imam finishes the extra), the follower's prayer is invalid. Also, it is makruh tahrimi for the follower to say salam after the imam's tashahhud but before the imam's salam, because that would mean following in tashahhud but not in salam.

Key Terms
المقتدي (al-muqtadi): The follower in congregational prayer who follows the imam.
قيدها (qayyadaha): To tie the extra rak'ah with a prostration, i.e., to complete it by performing the prostration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Say salam immediately to follow the imam.
B) Complete the tashahhud and then say salam.
C) Wait for the imam to return to tashahhud.
D) Leave the prayer.

Key Takeaway
The follower must complete his own wajib obligations and follow the imam in normal actions, but must never follow the imam's forgetful errors.

The Follower's Conduct When the Imam Precedes Him (Six Scenarios)
Rulings on the follower's conduct when imam leads ahead

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six rules governing the follower's actions when the imam errs and apply them to specific scenarios.

Core Idea
The follower must not exceed the imam, but must also not blindly follow an imam's mistake.

In congregational prayer, the follower (muqtadi) is bound to follow the imam. But what if the imam forgetfully adds an extra rak'ah, makes salam early, or stands up prematurely? The Hanafi school provides clear rules to preserve the integrity of the prayer while maintaining the principle of followership. This lesson unpacks six key scenarios and the follower's obligations in each.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Principle
The muqtadi must not precede the imam in actions, nor should he blindly follow an imam's clear mistake. He must use discernment.
This principle underlies all six rules.
2
Identify the Six Scenarios
Read the passage and list each scenario: (1) imam says salam early, (2) imam raises head before follower's tasbih, (3) imam adds a prostration or stands after final sitting, (4) imam returns before tying extra rak'ah, (5) imam ties extra rak'ah, (6) imam stands before final sitting.
Number the scenarios for clarity.
3
Determine the Follower's Obligation
For each scenario, state what the follower must do: complete tashahhud, follow immediately, wait, join salam, or pray alone.
Pay attention to the timing of invalidation.
4
Recognize Invalidating Actions
The passage explicitly states that if the follower makes salam before the imam ties the extra rak'ah, his prayer is invalid. Identify this boundary.
Also note the makruh tahrimi of leaving after the imam's tashahhud but before his salam.
5
Apply to New Scenarios
Using the six rules, reason through similar situations not explicitly mentioned, such as the imam adding an entire rak'ah without sitting.
The key is whether the extra action is 'tied' (i.e., completed) or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الإمامة
١٥٥
فصل
فيما يفعله المقتدي بعد فراغ إمامه من واجب وغيره
١ - لو سلَّم الإمام قبل فراغ المقتدي من التشهد : يُتِمُّه.
٢- ولو رفع الإمامُ رأسه قبل تسبيح المقتدي ثلاثاً في الركوع، أو السجود : يُتابعه
٣- ولو زاد الإمام سجدةً، أو قام بعد القعود الأخير ساهياً: لا يتبعه المؤتم، بل يمكث (۱).

٥- وإن قيدها (٢): سلَّم وحده.

(٢) أي الركعة الزائدة.
(۳) أي المأموم، وسبح له؛ ليتنبه.

Chapter of Imamate. Section: Regarding what the follower does after his imam finishes obligatory and other [actions]. 1. If the imam makes salam before the follower completes the tashahhud: he completes it. 2. If the imam raises his head before the follower's three tasbihs in bowing or prostration: he follows him. 3. If the imam adds a prostration, or stands up after the final sitting forgetfully: the follower does not follow him, but remains [waiting]. 4. If the imam returns before tying the extra [rak'ah] with a prostration: he makes salam with him. 5. If he ties it: he makes salam alone. 6. If the imam stands up before the final sitting forgetfully: he waits for him. If the follower makes salam before his imam ties the extra [rak'ah] with a prostration: his obligatory prayer is invalid. And disliked (with tahrim) is the salam of the follower after the imam's tashahhud before his salam.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage lays out six specific rules for the follower (muqtadi) when the imam's actions diverge from the normal sequence. The key principle is that the follower must not exceed the imam in performing actions, but if the imam omits or adds something forgetfully, the follower should not blindly follow him. The rulings are: (1) If the imam says salam before the follower finishes tashahhud, the follower completes his tashahhud and then follows. (2) If the imam raises his head from bowing or prostration before the follower completes three tasbihs, the follower immediately follows the imam; he does not complete the tasbihs. (3) If the imam adds an extra prostration or stands up after the final sitting forgetfully, the follower does not follow him but remains in his position, and he should say 'SubhanAllah' to alert the imam. (4) If the imam returns to the correct action before tying the extra rak'ah with a prostration (i.e., before making a second sajdah in that rak'ah), then the follower joins him in salam. (5) If the imam ties the extra rak'ah (i.e., completes two prostrations), then the follower should make salam alone; his prayer is complete. (6) If the imam stands up after the final sitting forgetfully (thinking he still has rak'ahs), the follower waits in the sitting position, alerting the imam by saying 'SubhanAllah'. The passage then states that if the follower makes salam before the imam ties the extra rak'ah, his prayer is invalid because he left the congregation improperly. Finally, it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) for the follower to make salam after the imam's tashahhud but before the imam's salam, because this would involve sitting alone for the final sitting, breaking the required followership.

Key Terms
المقتدي (al-muqtadi): The person praying behind an imam in congregation.
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The recitation of the testimony of faith (al-tahiyyat) in the sitting position of prayer.
تقييد الزائدة بسجدة (taqyid al-za'idah bi-sajdah): Tying the extra rak'ah by performing two prostrations (i.e., completing it as a full rak'ah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the imam adds an extra prostration forgetfully during the prayer, what should the follower do?
A) Perform the extra prostration as well.
B) Remain in his position and say SubhanAllah.
C) Make salam immediately.
D) Recite al-Fatihah again.

Key Takeaway
The follower must balance followership with preserving the validity of his prayer by completing wajib acts and not blindly following the imam's mistakes.

Post-Fard Adhkar and Imam's Conduct
Rulings on dhikr and imam's actions after fard

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended actions for the imam after the fard prayer and identify the Hanafi position on the connection between fard and sunnah.

Core Idea
The imam should briefly recite du'a, turn left, and face the congregation as they seek forgiveness three times.

This passage from Nur al-Idah outlines the sunnah practices following the obligatory prayer, focusing on the imam's conduct. It also presents a mild intra-Hanafi difference regarding whether to delay the sunnah by reciting awrad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings on connecting sunnah
The author (musannif) considers standing immediately for the sunnah after fard to be sunnah. However, Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani permits reciting awrad (litanies) between them, implying flexibility.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference; the mufta bihi is to recite a short du'a and then pray the sunnah.
2
Understand the imam's three recommended actions
After his salam, the imam should: (1) turn to his left side for the sunnah prayer, (2) face the congregation, and (3) the congregation says 'Astaghfirullah' three times.
The imam should avoid facing someone who is still praying.
3
Recognize the du'a after salam
The text from Maraqi recommends separating fard and sunnah by reciting: 'Allahumma anta al-salam...'
This du'a is brief and does not involve prolonged sitting.
4
Appreciate the additional view from al-Ikhtiyar
The author of al-Ikhtiyar considers it makruh to sit after the fard and make long du'a if a sunnah follows; instead one should immediately engage in the sunnah.
This view is more strict but the relied-upon position allows a brief separation.

And from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani: there is no harm in reciting awrad between the fard and the sunnah. And it is recommended (mustahabb) for the imam after his salam: 1. Commentary
This passage discusses the recommended practices after the obligatory (fard) prayer, especially concerning the transition to the sunnah prayer. The first ruling: standing immediately for the sunnah prayer after completing the fard is itself sunnah (recommended), meaning there should be minimal delay. However, Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, a prominent Hanafi scholar, stated that there is no harm in reciting some awrad (liturgical prayers or supplications) in between the fard and the sunnah, implying flexibility. The text then lists two mustahabb (recommended) actions for the imam after his salam: (1) to turn to his left side (or towards the qibla? actually 'to his left' to face the congregation? The text says 'to his left side for the supererogatory prayer' but that seems to imply he turns to face the people? The next point says 'to face the people' so it may be two separate actions: first to turn to his left (perhaps a slight turn) then to fully face them.) (2) To face the congregation after finishing the prayer, and the congregation recites 'Astaghfirullah' three times. The footnote from Maraqi elaborates that it is recommended to separate between fard and sunnah by reciting the specific du'a: 'Allahumma anta al-salam...' The commentary then notes a difference: the author (musannif) of Nur al-Idah considers connecting the sunnah without any gap as sunnah, but the author of al-Ikhtiyar says it is makruh (disliked) to sit after the fard and make long dua if there is a sunnah prayer, rather one should immediately engage in the sunnah. This is a mild ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school, with the preferred position being that a brief separation by the above du'a is better. The passage also includes a brief biography of al-Halwani, and a note that the imam should not face a person who is praying (to avoid disturbing him).

Key Terms
الأوراد (al-awrad): Liturgical prayers or litanies recited at specific times, here between fard and sunnah prayers.
السنة (al-sunnah): The supererogatory prayer associated with the obligatory prayer, e.g., the rawatib.
الاستغفار (al-istighfar): Seeking forgiveness from Allah by saying 'Astaghfirullah'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is to separate fard and sunnah by a brief du'a, then immediately pray the sunnah; the imam should turn to his left and the congregation should do istighfar.

Recommended Post-Fard Adhkar and the Connective Sunnah
Lists recommended post-fard prayer acts for imam and congregation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the recommended post-fard acts for the imam and congregation and explain the Hanafi ruling on connecting the sunnah prayer to the fard.

Core Idea
Post-fard dhikr is mustahabb, but the sunnah prayer should be started promptly after the fard.

This passage from Nur al-Idah outlines the recommended adhkar and actions after the obligatory prayer. It also addresses a key scholarly difference: whether the imam and congregation should sit for extended dhikr or immediately stand for the sunnah prayer. The Hanafi school emphasizes prompt connection but permits a brief pause for a specific du'a.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main issue: connecting sunnah to fard
The author states it is sunnah to stand immediately for the sunnah prayer after the fard. Note the difference with al-Halwani who permits reciting awrad between them.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
2
Note the intra-madhhab difference
Compare the author's view with al-Ikhtiyar who says it is makruh to sit for du'a after fard when a sunnah follows; you should occupy yourself with the sunnah instead.
The mufta bihi is the author's view: connecting is sunnah, but a brief pause for the taslim du'a is recommended.
3
List the imam's recommended actions after salam
The imam should turn to his left for the sunnah, face the people (if not blocking a praying person), and the congregation says 'Astaghfirullah' three times, then the imam recites the taslim du'a briefly.
4
List the congregation's adhkar sequence
Recite Ayat al-Kursi and al-Mu'awwidhat (Ikhlas, Falaq, Nas), then Subhanallah 33x, Alhamdulillah 33x, Allahu Akbar 33x, then say 'La ilaha illallah...' and make du'a raising hands and wiping face.
These are all mustahabb (recommended), not obligatory.
5
Understand the evidence for du'a and face-wiping
The hadith in Sunan al-Tirmidhi (3386) supports the du'a and wiping the face. The Hanafi school accepts this as evidence for the recommended practice, though its thubut and dalala are zanni.
The face-wiping after du'a is considered mustahabb based on this hadith.

Section: On the remembrances reported after the obligatory prayer.

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah outlines the recommended (sunnah and mustahabb) actions for the imam and congregation immediately after the obligatory (fard) prayer. It is a ruling statement—a concise list of post-prayer adhkar and etiquette.

The passage begins with a point of scholarly difference: the author states it is sunnah to stand immediately for the sunnah prayer after the fard, linking them consecutively. However, Shams al-Aimma al-Halwani permits reciting litanies (awrad) between them, indicating flexibility. The author’s own view is to connect them, but he also notes the recommended separation by saying the taslim (peace) prayer—'Allahumma antas-salam…'—as stated in Muraqi. This shows the Hanafi position that while connecting is sunnah, a brief pause for this du'a is recommended.

Then it lists specific recommended acts for the imam after his salam: turning to his left (to face the congregation or for supererogatory prayer), facing the people (if not blocking someone praying), and then the congregation saying 'Astaghfirullah' three times.

After the salam, the congregation recites Ayat al-Kursi and al-Mu'awwidhat (the last three surahs, with al-Samadiyyah meaning Surah al-Ikhlas). They then glorify Allah (Subhanallah), praise Him (Alhamdulillah), and magnify Him (Allahu Akbar) each 33 times—this totals 99, completing the one hundred with 'La ilaha illallah…' as specified. Finally, they make du'a raising hands and wipe their faces, which is a practice based on a hadith in Sunan al-Tirmidhi (deemed sahih gharib).

All these acts are mustahabb (recommended) in the Hanafi school, not obligatory. The footnotes clarify that 'Mu'awwidhat' includes al-Samadiyyah (Ikhlas) and the two Mu'awwidhat (Falaq and Nas). The hand-wiping after du'a is supported by the cited hadith, though some Hanafi scholars debate its strength. The overall lesson is to structure the post-fard time with specific dhikr and du'a, while being mindful of the imam's role and congregation's actions.

Key Terms
السنة (al-sunnah): In this passage, 'sunnah' refers to the supererogatory prayer (nafl) that is regularly performed after the fard prayer, such as the two rak'ahs after Zuhr, Maghrib, and Isha. It is distinct from the fard and highly recommended but not obligatory.
الاستغفار (al-istighfar): Seeking forgiveness from Allah by saying 'Astaghfirullah' three times after the fard prayer, as a means of acknowledging human shortcomings in worship.
المعوذات (al-mu'awwidhat): The protective surahs: al-Ikhlas (called al-Samadiyyah), al-Falaq, and al-Nas. The term predominates al-Mu'awwidhatan (the last two) to include al-Ikhlas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Post-fard adhkar are recommended but should not delay the sunnah prayer; the imam should turn left and start the sunnah promptly.
